But, she added, \u201cShe\u2019ll be O.K.\u201d\n\nAnd then Ms. Klassen expressed a sentiment that would be repeated by many as she left Scenic Mountain Medical Center. There was something familiar and reassuring to be found in continuing the football season. \u201cWe\u2019re still a team and we\u2019ll persevere,\u201d she said.\n\nHalee DeGraffenreid, 17, had been one of the more fortunate cheerleaders. She stood in the lobby of the medical center with a gash across her nose and her arm in a sling. She had needed stitches in her elbow but otherwise seemed O.K., her mother said.\n\nHalee had been listening to music and trying to sleep on the bus when she felt a sudden braking and hurtled forward. \u201cI heard a lot of crying and screaming,\u201d she said. \u201cEveryone was on top of each other. It was real scary.\u201d\n\nSuddenly, Halee felt queasy. She sat down in a wheelchair and doctors took her to an examining room before releasing her half an hour later, according to Mr. Baum, the principal.\n\nSometime after midnight, highlights from the Iraan-Munday game appeared on television in the hospital lobby. The Iraan fans turned their eyes to the screen but watched with blank stares. At about 3:30 a.m. on Saturday, the Pope and Garlock families left a small room off the lobby. Relatives hugged them and they cried without words.\n\nLate Saturday morning, the football players met at Iraan High School. They had spoken briefly on the phone.\n\nCoach Mark Kirchhoff gave other updates: Ms. Garlock had been stabilized overnight and doctors were operating on her legs. She was expected to recover. But what happens when these picky eaters grow up?\n\nMany children leave behind their quirky eating habits as they get older. But \u201cwhat is less certain are the predictors of who will remain a picky eater in adulthood,\u201d said Nancy Zucker, a psychologist and director of the Duke Center for Eating Disorders. She is the lead author of a 2015 study that linked picky eating in some preschoolers with emotional problems.\n\nAdults may hang on to their youthful food dislikes \u2014 at the top of the list are vegetables \u2014 but then add more restrictions, such as consuming only smooth foods or limiting their diet to bland, white meals. Dr. Zucker said that in a sample of 2,600 adults who identified themselves as picky eaters, 75 percent reported the pattern started in childhood.\n\nThe estimates of picky eating in childhood vary widely, ranging from 5 percent to more than 25 percent, depending on the definition of selective eating. But \u201cthere is no consensus on the prevalence in adults,\u201d Dr. Zucker said. One reason is that many adults try to keep their picky eating a secret, a common source of stress that can lead to personal or professional problems.\n\nAs a child, Stephanie Lucianovic, the author of \u201cSuffering Succotash: A Picky Eater\u2019s Quest to Understand Why We Hate the Foods We Hate,\u201d would hide vegetables or hold her nose during meals. As an adult, her selective eating posed new challenges when she fell in love with a man from a \u201cfoodie\u201d family.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s a really scary thing to overcome,\u201d she said. \u201cPeople aren\u2019t choosing to dislike food. There\u2019s a lot of shame involved. There\u2019s not a lot of empathy for picky eaters.\u201d\n\nMs. Lucianovic said the key to ending her pickiness at the table was having the support of a nonjudgmental partner and learning how to cook. She still avoids steamed vegetables, but now enjoys them roasted or saut\u00e9ed. \u201cYou can reset the pattern of likes and dislikes,\u201d she said. \u201cI did it by continuing to try things in different ways.\u201d\n\nResearchers at Duke and the University of Pittsburgh are studying adult picky eaters via an online questionnaire. Among the questions: \u201cDo you get anxious about social situations because you will be expected to eat?\u201d and \u201cDo you gag when tasting a disliked food?\u201d About 40,000 adults have completed the survey.\n\nThe causes of long-term picky eating, however, remain poorly understood. In some cases, a childhood dining scare from choking or vomiting may cause lingering fear of one or more foods. \u201cFood memories are very powerful memories,\u201d Dr. Zucker said, adding that social anxiety associated with picky eating seems to increase with age.\n\nOther people may have a heightened sensitivity \u201cor even somewhat distorted perception to certain tastes and smells,\u201d said Juyun Lim, an associate professor of food science and technology at Oregon State University whose research has focused on the role of the senses in food preferences.\n\nAll foods contain tens to hundreds of volatile compounds that determine aroma and flavor, she said. But the same food can be perceived differently by two individuals.\n\n\u201cDepending on your genetics, a food can smell pleasant or unpleasant,\u201d Dr. Lim said. While one person may enjoy the taste of cilantro, for example, another might find it soapy. Another example is pork, which contains a compound that some people can\u2019t detect, or describe as pleasantly floral, while others characterize it as smelling like sweat or urine.\n\nSometimes we learn to like something the more we are exposed to it. \u201cThe first time you drink beer, it never really tastes good,\u201d Dr. Lim said.\n\nTexture also plays a role in food acceptance. One example is okra, with its slimy quality that many find disagreeable. Jane Kauer, an anthropologist at the University of Pennsylvania, was surprised when research revealed that some adults rejected raw tomatoes for reasons that had nothing to do with flavor. Participants described being turned off by the \u201cgross thing that happens when you pierce a tomato and the guts come out of it.\u201d\n\nMany adult picky eaters want to change, but they find certain foods too unappealing to even put on a plate. In extreme cases, they may shun nearly all foods, a condition the American Psychiatric Association calls avoidant/restrictive food intake disorder, or Arfid.\n\nDr. Zucker said some adult picky eaters come to the Duke center for help when they are concerned about being poor role models for their children, for example, or they panic about attending business dinners. Psychologists and social workers help patients gain insight into the biological, emotional and social factors behind their selective eating.\n\n\u201cA person\u2019s experiences often need to be validated and fully understood before changes are even considered,\u201d Dr. Zucker said. New foods are then slowly introduced in an effort to expand the palate.\n\nDr. Zucker compared the process to physical rehabilitation for an injury, requiring much work and practice. Patients who have eaten only smooth foods may, for example, need to consult with an occupational therapist to learn how to chew and swallow more effectively. They are also taught ways of handling situations like eating in public or explaining their food preferences to others.\n\nOne thing experts advise: Don\u2019t push food at adult picky eaters. \u201cIt\u2019s incredibly stressful to them, because most of us are perfectly happy to say, \u2018Come on, try it. It really tastes great,\u2019\u201d Dr. Kauer said.\n\nWhile loved ones\u2019 concern is understandable, selective eaters consider it intrusive and an attack on their sense of self, Dr. Kauer said. \u201cIt\u2019s getting into their space, and it has nothing to do with you.\u201d\n", "squander lead knicks win early games kings prevail eighth": "\nJeff Hornacek, the Knicks\u2019 coach, has recently become a believer in the philosophy that less is more. Instead of peppering his players\u2019 minds with repeated reminders of their flaws, he has decided that ignoring the shortcomings may be best. After all, he says, the Knicks were poor free-throw shooters until they finally just stopped talking about it.\n\nNow, he is hoping that ignorance can be the Knicks\u2019 weapon against their newest foible. For the fourth consecutive game, New York let a sizable lead evaporate. It has become a nightly question hanging over the Knicks\u2019 heads for the last week: How large a lead did they need to avoid blowing it and then scrambling to hold onto a win? Each night, they have failed to provide an answer.\n\nAn 11-point first-quarter advantage over the Thunder on Monday was not large enough. Up by 17 points in the fourth quarter in Minneapolis on Wednesday, they needed a last-second lifesaver from Carmelo Anthony. Trump the presidency.\n\nPresident Vladimir V. Putin\u2019s goal in Europe is to radicalize politics, analysts say, breaking the liberal consensus and strengthening his regional influence.\n\nAs early as April, Mr. Renzi complained privately to his counterparts about Russia meddling in his country\u2019s politics by supporting anti-establishment parties.\n\nIn November, he privately discussed the spread of fake news with other European leaders and President Obama at a meeting in Berlin. Mr. Obama forcefully assailed the phenomenon while standing beside Angela Merkel, the German chancellor, who has warned of Russian misinformation campaigns that \u201ccould play a role\u201d in her own re-election bid.\n\nMr. Renzi\u2019s private concerns were relayed by several current and former officials with knowledge of the conversations who were not authorized to speak publicly.\n\nHis biggest domestic opponent has been the surging and anti-establishment Five Star Movement, which would benefit most should he lose Sunday\u2019s referendum on streamlining Italy\u2019s bureaucracy.\n\nLaura Boldrini, the speaker of the lower house of Parliament, said she had no information about whether the Five Star Movement trafficked in fake news or promulgated Russian propaganda. In a new study, more than half of chronic insomniacs who used an automated online therapy program reported improvement within weeks and were sleeping normally a year later.\n\nThe new report, published in the journal JAMA Psychiatry, is the most comprehensive to date suggesting that many garden-variety insomniacs could benefit from the gold standard treatment \u2014 cognitive behavior therapy \u2014 without ever having to talk to a therapist. At least one in 10 adults has diagnosable insomnia, which is defined as broken, irregular, inadequate slumber at least three nights a week for three months running or longer.\n\n\u201cI\u2019ve been an insomniac all my life, I\u2019ve tried about everything,\u201d said Dale Love Callon, 70, known as Dacie, a math tutor living in Rancho Palos Verdes, Calif., who recently used the software. \u201cI don\u2019t have it 100 percent conquered, but I\u2019m sleeping much better now.\u201d\n\nPrevious studies have found that online sleep therapy can be effective, but most have been smaller, or focused on a particular sleep-related problem, like depression. The new trial tested the digital therapy in a broad, diverse group of longtime insomniacs whose main complaint was lack of sleep. Most had used medication or supplements over the years, and some still did.\n\n\u201cThese results suggest that there are a group of patients who can benefit without the need of a high-intensity intervention,\u201d like face-to-face therapy, said Jack Edinger, a professor in the department of medicine at National Jewish Health in Denver, who was not a part of the study. \u201cWe don\u2019t know yet exactly who they are \u2014 the people who volunteer for a study like this in first place are self-motivated \u2014 but they\u2019re out there.\u201d\n\nIn the study, led by researchers at the University of Virginia, doctors recruited 303 people ages 21 to 65 over the internet. Half were randomly assigned to receive education and advice on insomnia \u2014 a digital \u201cplacebo,\u201d of sorts, though an active one, in that such advice often helps people sleep better. The other half got a six-week focused online therapy product, called SHUTi.\n\nSome of the researchers, as well as the university, have a stake in this product, which costs $135 for 16 weeks of access. None of those connected to the company analyzed the data or had access to it, or participated in the data analysis, said Lee Ritterband, the lead author and a developer of the online therapy.\n\nSHUTi is not the only digital insomnia therapy product on the market. Sleepio, which costs $300 for a year\u2019s access, and is offered by a London-based company, also incorporates cognitive therapy. And it was also found in a randomized study to have good results.\n\nBoth incorporate the techniques of cognitive behavior therapy for insomnia, an approach therapists have been using successfully for years. Some of those techniques date back decades. One is called sleep restriction, in which people set a regular \u201csleep window\u201d and work to stick to it. Another is called stimulus control, an attempt to break the association between lying in bed and activities like streaming video and eating.\n\nFinally, the therapy aims to undermine self-defeating assumptions about sleep, like \u201cWithout a good night\u2019s sleep, I can\u2019t function the next day\u201d or \u201cMedication is probably the only solution to sleeplessness.\u201d The program prompts people to log in daily and record each night\u2019s sleep in some detail; it then tailors weekly sessions based on those entries.\n\nMs. Love Callon\u2019s problem, for example, still wakes up in the middle of the night, at 4 a.m. or thereabouts. The online program, she said, instructed her to get out of bed when that happened, and sit and read for 40 minutes \u2014 which is more likely to induce sleepiness than, say, shopping online. \u201cAnd it has worked,\u201d she said. \u201cI get drowsy while reading and have been able to go back to bed and fall asleep.\u201d\n\nThe research team tracked the participants, assessing their sleep quality every several months, using standardized questionnaires. After a year, 57 percent of the people using the online therapy program were sleeping normally, compared with 27 percent of those who had gotten only advice and education.\n\n\u201cWe continued to see improvement from the six-month assessment through the end of the year, even though people had stopped using the program,\u201d Dr. Ritterband said. \u201cSo, that\u2019s a very good sign.\u201d\n\nAccording to Dr. John Torous, co-director of the digital psychiatry program at Beth Israel Deaconess Medical Center and Harvard Medical School, there are about a dozen online programs on the market using cognitive behavior therapy techniques \u2014 for a variety of conditions, including depression \u2014 which also have rigorous evidence behind them.\n\n\u201cThere are maybe ten thousand or so mental apps out there, and the number is increasing way faster than the evidence base,\u201d Dr. Torous said, \u201cso it\u2019s good to see someone doing careful studies.\u201d\n\nDr. Torous said the one caveat for all of them is adherence. \u201cWhen you stop paying people to be in a study, when they stop getting reminder phone calls, they often stop doing it,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s like a gym membership that way; people may do it twice and then let it go.\u201d\n\nNonetheless, the potential of online therapies to reach huge numbers of people makes it likely that they will become first-line therapy in many cases, some experts say.\n\n\u201cDespite reasons for restraint, it seems inevitable that internet CBT-I will be increasingly used as a first-line insomnia intervention,\u201d concluded an editorial accompanying the study in the journal. \u201cIt also seems likely that the medical community may have little influence on whether, when, and how this occurs.\u201d\n", "late apologizes critics mea haitis cholera culpa disease general brought mention role kimoon ban outbreak haiti avoided secretary": "\nUNITED NATIONS \u2014 After six years and 10,000 deaths, the United Nations issued a carefully worded public apology on Thursday for its role in the 2010 cholera outbreak in Haiti and the widespread suffering it has caused since then.\n\nThe mea culpa, which Secretary General Ban Ki-moon delivered before the General Assembly, avoided any mention of who brought cholera to Haiti, even though the disease was not present in the country until United Nations peacekeepers arrived from Nepal, where an outbreak was underway.\n\nThe peacekeepers lived on a base that often leaked waste into a river, and the first cholera cases in the country appeared in Haitians who lived nearby. Numerous scientists have long argued that the base was the source of the outbreak, but for years United Nations officials refused to accept responsibility.\n\nEven though Mr. Ban\u2019s office has acknowledged that the United Nations had played a role in the outbreak, his apology on Thursday was limited to how the world body responded to the outbreak, not how it started.\n\n\u201cWe simply did not do enough with regard to the cholera outbreak and its spread in Haiti,\u201d Mr. Ban said on Thursday. \u201cWe are profoundly sorry about our role.\u201d\n\nApologies are rare for the United Nations. In 1999, Mr. Ban\u2019s predecessor, Kofi Annan, expressed \u201cdeep remorse\u201d for the organization\u2019s failure to protect civilians from the genocide in Rwanda five years earlier.\n\nMr. Ban\u2019s apology \u2014 belated in the view of his critics \u2014 is part of his push for redress in Haiti before the end of his 10-year tenure on Dec. 31. Yet the people of Haiti have seen few tangible benefits so far.\n\nThe United Nations has not yet met its promise to eradicate cholera once and for all from Haiti, though Mr. Ban\u2019s aides said on Thursday that they were close to raising the $200 million they say they need to fix Haiti\u2019s water and sanitation system and treat Haitians for cholera. Nor has the United Nations yet raised an additional $200 million it wants for \u201cmaterial assistance\u201d to families and communities that have suffered; donor nations have not yet come forward with the funds.\n\n\u201cIt is aimed at providing a meaningful \u2014 but necessarily imperfect \u2014 response to the impact of cholera on individuals, families and communities,\u201d Mr. Ban said in a report made public on Thursday.\n\nMr. Ban goaded countries to make \u201cvoluntary contributions,\u201d or else other means would have to be pursued, which can be interpreted only to be compulsory dues. \u201cEliminating cholera from Haiti, and living up to our moral responsibility to those who have been most directly affected, will require the full commitment of the international community and, crucially, the resources necessary,\u201d he said.\n\nThe Haitian ambassador to the United Nations, Denis Regis, welcomed Mr. Ban\u2019s acknowledgment of the organization\u2019s role in the cholera outbreak, calling its previous position \u201cmorally unjustifiable.\u201d\n\nFew things have damaged the standing of the United Nations \u2014 and Mr. Ban\u2019s record as its secretary general \u2014 as the cholera outbreak and particularly, his response to it.\n\nIn a country where few people have access to clean water and proper sanitation, the disease quickly spread in 2010 when United Nations peacekeepers failed to adequately follow protocols for disposing wastewater, while deployed in Haiti. Cholera surged again in the aftermath of Hurricane Matthew, which hit the nation in October.\n\nOne of the reasons the disease spread so widely, public health experts have said, is because it was allowed to; had there been a vigorous response in the first couple of years, it would have been far easier to contain, and fewer people would have died. The death toll stands at an estimated 10,000; some say it could be higher.\n\nIn the report issued on Thursday, Mr. Ban blamed perennial underfunding for cholera eradication. \u201cEfforts were beset from the start by the challenge of insufficient funding, which has had a dramatic negative impact on the capacity to respond effectively to the disease,\u201d he said.\n\nPerhaps most damaging to the United Nations, which regularly presses governments around the world to pursue accountability, Mr. Ban and his aides have all along claimed immunity from prosecution under a longstanding diplomatic treaty. That view was upheld in August by a federal court in Manhattan, in response to a class-action lawsuit by cholera victims.\n\nIn recent months, Mr. Ban has expressed \u201cmoral responsibility\u201d for the outbreak and \u201cdeep regret.\u201d Senior United Nations officials said they had been constrained from saying any more by their lawyers, who were concerned that it would make them vulnerable to further legal claims elsewhere over misconduct or mistakes by its peacekeepers.\n\nMr. Ban made the apology in English, French and Haitian Creole. It was delicately worded to avoid the impression that the United Nations was taking full responsibility for cholera in Haiti. That could imply legal culpability.\n\nThe group that represents the victims, the Institute for Justice and Democracy in Haiti, has said it has not yet decided on whether to take the matter to the United States Supreme Court to seek compensation.\n\nBrian Concannon, the executive director of the group, called Mr. Ban\u2019s proposals a step in the right direction. \u201cBut words alone won\u2019t save lives or remove the stain on the U.N.\u2019s reputation,\u201d he said in an email. \u201cThe U.N. and its member states will have to rise to the challenge and actually fund and effectively execute the projects.\u201d\n\nMr. Ban\u2019s aides have said they would prefer to use the material assistance funds to aid communities most affected by the cholera outbreak \u2014 by giving children of survivors a free education, for instance \u2014 rather than open up a fund for individual claims for compensation. Trump\u2019s surprise victory at the feet of disaffected white working-class voters in the Rust Belt \u2014 or more specifically, the failure of coastal elites to understand them.\n\nUnto the breach steps Edward McClelland\u2019s \u201cHow to Speak Midwestern,\u201d a dictionary wrapped in some serious dialectology inside a gift book trailing a serious whiff of Relevance.\n\nNot that Mr. McClelland, a native of Lansing, Mich., who now lives in Chicago, set out to write a topical book. Hillary Clinton\u2019s name appears only once, when he notes that he stumped for Bernie Sanders in Iowa but ultimately voted for Mrs. Clinton \u201cbecause we haven\u2019t had a president with an Inland North accent since Gerald Ford.\u201d\n\nStill, the book can be read as questioning the resurgent notion \u2014 perhaps strongest in the Midwest itself \u2014 that Midwesterners are the most authentically American of Americans, starting with their allegedly neutral speech.\n\n\u201cAccents are an important element of regional identity,\u201d Mr. McClelland writes. \u201cAnd an important element of Midwestern identity is believing you don\u2019t have an accent.\u201d\n\nFull disclosure: Like Mrs. Clinton, I\u2019m a white woman who grew up in the Chicago suburbs. When it comes to pinched nasal vowels and strongly pronounced r\u2019s (a phenomenon linguists call rhoticity), I\u2019m With Her.\n\nThat \u201cwhite\u201d part is important. The heavily industrialized (and segregated) Inland North \u2014 as dialectologists call the region stretching roughly from central New York across the Great Lakes \u2014 \u201chas a wider divergence between white and black speech than anywhere in the country,\u201d Mr. McClelland writes, with African-Americans largely maintaining speech patterns brought from the South. (Mr. McClelland notes the existence of various Midwestern \u201cblaccents,\u201d though he doesn\u2019t explore them.)\n\n\u201cHow to Speak Midwestern\u201d is a product of Belt Publishing, a three-year-old Cleveland start-up that promotes a kind of progressive Rust Belt pride without succumbing to clich\u00e9 or hipster irony. In addition to producing an online magazine, Belt has put out anthologies dedicated to Akron, Buffalo, Pittsburgh, Youngstown and other cities, along with titles like \u201cHow to Live in Detroit Without Being a Jackass.\u201d\n\nMr. McClelland, whose previous books include \u201cYoung Mr. Obama: Chicago and the Making of a Black President\u201d and \u201cNothin\u2019 but Blue Skies: The Heyday, Hard Times and Hopes of America\u2019s Industrial Heartland,\u201d takes a pan-Midwestern approach, offering a sweeping consideration of the broad forces that have shaped the region\u2019s speech, as well as glossaries dedicated to 11 cities, states or subregions.\n\nReaders will learn the lingo of Yinzers (Pittsburghers), Cheeseheads (Wisconsinites), Baja Minnesotans (as those from the Land of 10,000 Lakes derisively call people in Iowa) and Michiganders (a coinage often attributed to Abraham Lincoln, who used it as an insult during the 1848 presidential campaign). But in addition to Midwestern, they will also pick up a good bit of Linguist.\n\nMonophthongization? That\u2019s the turning of double-stepped vowels into a single sound, as in Steeler fans waving their \u201cTerrible Tahhls\u201d (towels) as they head \u201cdahntahn\u201d (downtown).\n\nFinal obstruent devoicing? The tendency to pronounce final consonants without vibrating the vocal cords, made famous by the \u201cSaturday Night Live\u201d skit \u201cBill Swerski\u2019s Superfans\u201d and its paeans to \u201cda Bearsss\u201d (rather than \u201cda Bearzzz\u201d).\n\nLinguists divide the Midwest into three main regions, whose durable speech patterns reflect different waves of European settlement. The Inland North, initially settled by Yankees heading west from New England, is the most consistent region, with an accent that largely sounds the same from Rochester to Milwaukee. (It\u2019s also a region whose speech pulled subtly away from the rest of the country during the 20th century, thanks to a mysterious phenomenon known as the Northern Cities Vowel Shift.) The Midlands, which stretches from Pennsylvania and Ohio west to Kansas and Nebraska, shows much greater dialect diversity among cities, thanks to the clan-based social structure of the Scots-Irish, who began arriving via the port cities of the Mid-Atlantic States.\n\nAnd then there\u2019s the North Central region (Minnesota, Northern Wisconsin and the Upper Peninsula of Michigan), which has a strong German and Scandinavian influence, though few people, Mr. McClelland reports, actually sound like characters in \u201cFargo.\u201d For superior Minnesotan, Mr. McClelland prefers the \u201cclipped, brisk, nasal\u201d speech of Kurt Russell in the hockey movie \u201cMiracle.\u201d\n\nMr. McClelland\u2019s introductory chapters on these three regions dangle plenty of fascinating tidbits. For example, studies have shown that working-class women show less pronounced regional accents than their husbands, perhaps because of greater contact with doctors, teachers and other professionals.\n\nAnd who knew that the lack of verb endings in Minnesota\u2019s remote Iron Range might be traceable to the polyglot immigrant mineworkers who, in a region lacking a base of native English speakers, had to cook up a mutually intelligible pidgin?\n\nThe glossaries, on the other hand, may come as a bit of a letdown to old-school wordniks. As a devotee of The Dictionary of American Regional English, I was disappointed to find Mr. McClelland\u2019s glossaries light on entries like \u201cdevil strip\u201d (as the grassy area between the sidewalk and the street is known in Akron, Ohio) and \u201cn\u2019 at\u201d (a Pittsburghism, short for \u201cand that,\u201d which is tacked on to the end of a sentence to mean \u201cet cetera\u201d).\n\nInstead, Mr. McClelland offers lots of place nicknames, sports-related slang and local delicacies. Not that they aren\u2019t useful: I, for one, plan to seek out mettwurst, a homemade cold-smoked ring sausage, next time I\u2019m in western Iowa. But the word lists come off less as language guides than cheery tourist-bureau advice.\n\nSo, whither the Midwestern tongue? One scholar Mr. McClellan interviews sees an erosion of its specific varietals, as young people identify more as Midwesterners than as Chicagoans or Buffalonians. Others see a weakening of the Northern Cities Vowel Shift.\n\nAs for Mrs. Clinton, in addition to heeding other portents, she might have taken note of the claim, cited here, that her native Inland North is losing linguistic dominance to the Midlands, which has become the \u201cgreat swing region of American accents.\u201d\n\nAs the Scandinavians in my Midwestern family tree might have put it (using an allegedly Norwegian term that, Mr. McClelland writes, is \u201cseldom heard in Norway\u201d): Uff da!\n", "directed deny climate accuses founder fiercest fire companys company descendants rockefellers conspiracy change threat scheming return mobil accused": "\nExxon Mobil, under fire over its past efforts to undercut climate science, is accusing the Rockefeller family of masterminding a conspiracy against it. Yes, that Rockefeller family.\n\nThe company, which has been accused of scheming to pay surrogates to deny the threat of climate change, is trying to turn the tables by calling its opponents the real conspirators. It is fighting state attorneys general, journalists and environmental groups in an all-out campaign to defend its image.\n\nBut the oil and gas giant has directed some of its fiercest fire at the descendants of John D. Rockefeller, who in 1870 founded Standard Oil, the company that became Exxon Mobil. Rockefeller family charities, longtime backers of environmental causes, have supported much of the research and reporting that has called the company to account for its climate policies, and Exxon Mobil is crying foul.\n\nThe pressure on the company is intense. Journalists have published expos\u00e9s of the company\u2019s research into climate change, including actions it took to incorporate climate projections into its exploration plans while playing down the threat. Such reporting projects, financed in part by Rockefeller family charities, included last year\u2019s work by Inside Climate News and the Columbia University Graduate School of Journalism, which published its results with The Los Angeles Times. The findings have been boiled down to the popular Twitter shorthand #ExxonKnew.\n\nExxon Mobil, in public statements, court filings and thick dossiers on the company\u2019s opponents, says it is the target of a well-funded and politically motivated conspiracy to harm its core business.\n\nYet where Exxon Mobil and its allies see a tangled conspiracy, members of the Rockefeller family see an effort to use the vast wealth generated by fossil fuels to combat the damage done by fossil fuels.\n\nNow the family has taken the unusual step of going public to state its case in a rare interview and in a two-part essay in The New York Review of Books that lays out in detail Exxon Mobil\u2019s research and funding of climate denial. David Kaiser, an author of the essay and a fifth-generation Rockefeller, said dryly, \u201cThe family generally doesn\u2019t do public things in this way.\u201d\n\nHe said he was aware that the \u201cobvious historical irony of the fact that we are Rockefellers doing this would attract additional attention to the story \u2014 and we want attention to the story, because we think it will make clear to the public that the so-called debate over climate science has been a fake one, artificially manufactured, and a basically dishonest one from the beginning.\u201d\n\nState attorneys general, beginning last year with Eric T. The federal Securities and Exchange Commission has launched its own investigation of the company\u2019s accounting of reserves.\n\nExxon Mobil says it has recognized the threat of climate change and the need to fight it for more than a decade; the company says it stopped funding the organizations that promote climate denial in the mid-2000s. When the family fund made its announcement in March, it denounced Exxon Mobil\u2019s \u201cmorally reprehensible conduct\u201d on climate change.\n\nMr. Jeffers responded, \u201cIt\u2019s not surprising that they\u2019re divesting from the company since they\u2019re already funding a conspiracy against us.\u201d\n\nThe company and its allies have turned up the heat on its founding family and other opponents.\n\nIndustry-backed policy groups like Energy in Depth generate stories that attack the family and its philanthropy. Their charges are echoed in conservative news outlets like The Wall Street Journal's opinion page and The Daily Caller. Breitbart News has called the collaboration among environmental groups to urge the investigation of Exxon a \u201cRICO conspiracy,\u201d using the acronym for the federal racketeering law, and the industry-oriented site Natural Gas Now published an article declaring, \u201cIt\u2019s time to RICO the Rockefellers.\u201d\n\nThe company\u2019s allies offer journalists what political operatives refer to as opposition research, including court records and favorable articles (as do activist groups opposing Exxon Mobil).\n\nExxon Mobil has also pulled the Rockefeller philanthropies into its legal battles against the attorneys general investigating it, sending the groups a subpoena demanding documents and communications related to their activism.\n\nA federal judge in Dallas, Ed Kinkeade, granted the company the right to pursue discovery in its cases against the state attorneys general. He ruled that comments by Attorney General Maura Healey of Massachusetts at a news conference about the investigations in March, which also featured former Vice President Al Gore, may have shown that she was presupposing what an investigation would show, which may demonstrate that she acted in bad faith in pursuing the case. The judge has taken the exceptional step of ordering depositions of Ms. Healey and Mr. Schneiderman.\n\nThe Rockefeller funds have also received subpoenas from another friend of Exxon Mobil, the chairman of the House Science Committee, Rep. Lamar S. Smith, a Texas Republican. Mr. Smith has harshly criticized the attorneys general over their investigations, and has accused the Rockefeller funds of taking part in \u201ca coordinated effort to deprive companies, nonprofit organizations and scientists of their First Amendment rights and ability to fund and conduct scientific research free from intimidation and threats of prosecution.\u201d\n\nThe Rockefeller family responds that it is trying to right a historic social and environmental wrong, using the straightforward tools of the First Amendment.\n\n\u201cThe Rockefeller Family Fund has exercised its freedom of speech in expressing our repugnance at Exxon Mobil\u2019s behavior,\u201d said Mr. Kaiser, the president of the fund. \u201cWe have exercised our freedom of association by talking with like-minded public interest advocates about how best to educate the public about the realities of climate change. The engagement with Exxon itself has been more gradual; members of the family tried for more than a decade, through conversations with company executives and through proxy measures, to get Exxon to take a stronger stance in favor of clean energy and, in the days when the company was expressing doubts in paid advertisements in The New York Times about climate science, to get it to stop.\n\nPeter Frumhoff, the director of science and policy for the Union of Concerned Scientists, an advocacy group that published its own investigation of Exxon Mobil\u2019s efforts to promote uncertainty about climate science, said that his group had received \u201cmodest funding support\u201d over the years from the Rockefeller funds. So it was a surprise to Vanguard, an investment management company, when it found its ads on Breitbart News, the hard-right site that has become closely tied to President-elect Donald J. Trump.\n\nAn email from a client alerted Vanguard to the issue last week, prompting it to pull its ads from the site. Breitbart had been inadvertently included on a list of preapproved websites the company and its ad agency use to try to reach people who have visited Vanguard\u2019s site, said Emily Farrell, a spokeswoman.\n\nVanguard is one of at least a dozen companies, including prominent brands like Allstate, Kellogg and Warby Parker, that have said recently that they will stop advertising on Breitbart. The brands have attributed their appearances on the site to the automated nature of online advertising, in which a complicated system of third-party networks and agencies is used to place ads. That has cast a spotlight on how the zeal to capitalize on consumer data and advanced targeting technologies has resulted in some companies\u2019 being associated with sites they want to avoid.\n\nIt is increasingly rare for marketers and their agencies to place ads directly on websites. Unlike advertising on television or in magazines, where brands often choose placements in advance based on content, marketers online deploy sophisticated technologies to target and personalize ads in real time for the people who are most likely to be interested in their products or services, wherever they may be roaming on the web.\n\nKris Charles, a Kellogg representative, said that while the company reviewed sites for potential ad placements \u201cusing filtering technology to assess the words and phrases that make up a site\u2019s content,\u201d there is also \u201ca very large volume of websites, so occasionally something is inadvertently missed.\u201d\n\nAll of this has resulted in the kind of publicity \u2014 and political repercussions \u2014 brands were hoping to avoid. After Kellogg said it would stop advertising on Breitbart because the site was not \u201caligned with our values as a company,\u201d pointing to publicly available marketing guidelines, Breitbart started a #DumpKelloggs boycott campaign. Larry Solov, Breitbart\u2019s chief executive, said in an editorial that the cereal maker \u201chas shown its contempt for Breitbart\u2019s 45 million readers and for the main street American values that they hold dear.\u201d\n\nA spokeswoman for Breitbart did not respond to requests for comment for this article.\n\nAllstate responded to one consumer\u2019s complaint about its appearance on Breitbart by saying, \u201cUnfortunately, the nature of internet media buys is such that we are not always able to receive full disclosure with regards to all of the websites on which our advertising may run,\u201d though it would exclude Breitbart from its ad buys in the future. The eyeglass company Warby Parker blamed \u201cthird-party ad networks or ad exchanges,\u201d and affirmed its commitment to diversity and inclusivity. Other brands, including Nissan, have said they aim to reach as many customers as possible and do not plan to change their advertising strategy based on complaints from some consumers.\n\nThe nuances of how a brand ends up on a website, however, are often meaningless to consumers who may associate the two regardless of whether they have a direct relationship \u2014 which is being highlighted anew as tensions run high after the election.\n\nAbout two weeks ago, a Twitter account called \u201cSleeping Giants\u201d was created with the goal of choking off ad dollars going to Breitbart, urging people to screenshot ads on the site, then post those pictures and flag the advertisers. This process is known as blacklisting, and because of the sheer number of websites, brands tend to value its efficiency over any qualms they may have about appearing in the wrong place at the wrong time.\n\n\u201cThe blacklist approach starts with 500 billion impressions a day across the globe in real time across all screens and all formats; the white list approach starts with zero,\u201d said Joe Zawadzki, the chief executive of MediaMath, a marketing technology company. \u201cSo it\u2019s, do you start with the universe, then filter, or start with nothing, then build up?\u201d\n\nTo some, that illustrates the underlying problem. Spencer was suspended from Twitter recently, he hopped over to YouTube to address his supporters. \u201cDigitally speaking,\u201d he said, Twitter had sent \u201cexecution squads across the alt-right.\u201d He accused Twitter of \u201cpurging people on the basis of their views,\u201d calling it \u201ccorporate Stalinism.\u201d Then he mapped out a path forward. \u201cThere\u2019s obviously Gab, which is an interesting medium,\u201d he said. \u201cI think that will be the place where we go next.\u201d\n\nGab is a new social network built like a hybrid of Twitter and Reddit \u2014 posts are capped at 300 characters, and the crowd votes to boost or demote posts in the feed. But Gab\u2019s defining feature is its user guidelines, or rather, its lack thereof. Gab bans illegal activities \u2014 child pornography, threats of violence, terrorism \u2014 and not much else. \u201cFacebook, Twitter and Reddit are taking the path of censorship,\u201d Utsav Sanduja, Gab\u2019s chief communications officer, told me via email. \u201cGab does not.\u201d\n\nThink of Gab as the Make America Great Again of social sites: It\u2019s a throwback to the freewheeling norms of the old internet, before Twitter started cracking down on harassment and Reddit cleaned out its darkest corners. And since its debut in August, it has emerged as a digital safe space for the far right, where white nationalists, conspiracy-theorist YouTubers, and minivan majority moms can gather without liberal interference.\n\nThis election laid bare the ideological divide on social media, and since the election, the rift has deepened. Just as dejected Hillary Clinton supporters have come together in Pantsuit Nation \u2014 a \u201csecret\u201d Facebook group of nearly four million members \u2014 some on the right have found their postelection online oasis in the invitation-only Gab.\n\nGab\u2019s 25-year-old founder, Andrew Torba, dreamed up the site after reading reports that Facebook employees suppress conservative articles on the site. Mr. Torba \u2014 who previously created Kuhcoon, a system for running automated Facebook ad campaigns (it\u2019s now called Automate Ads) \u2014 is a rare conservative Christian tech C.E.O. Gab is a corrective to what he dubs \u201cBig Social,\u201d and it\u2019s based on what the company calls \u201ca pluralistic ethos of mutual respect and toleration of dissonant views.\u201d\n\nWhen other social sites push out disruptive users, Gab opens its arms. Recently, Twitter beefed up abuse rules to police not only threats but also hate speech \u201cagainst a race, religion, gender, or orientation.\u201d (The move presaged the purge that swept up Mr. Spencer.) And Reddit erased a community called Pizzagate, where conspiracy theorists had gathered to spin lies about Democratic pedophiles operating out of a D.C. pizzeria. On Gab, the topic is always trending.\n\nAll the big-name Twitter castaways have resurfaced here: In addition to Mr. Spencer, there is Milo Yiannopoulos, the Breitbart editor who was barred from Twitter for siccing trolls on the \u201cGhostbusters\u201d actress Leslie Jones; Pax Dickinson, the former Business Insider chief technology officer who rebranded himself as a victim of P.C. culture when he was sacked for posting sexist tweets; and Tila Tequila, the reality TV star who was booted from Twitter after posting racial slurs and pro-Nazi stuff. (In New York, \u201cdowntown\u201d dance includes Brooklyn.) With fabulous hubris, the choreographer Zvi Gotheiner, in his new piece, \u201cOn the Road,\u201d has tried rendering Jack Kerouac\u2019s 1957 novel as dance theater. With equal daring, the dancer-choreographer Caleb Teicher has set his new tap dance, \u201cMeet Ella,\u201d to recordings of Ella Fitzgerald at her most incandescent, live in Rome (1958) and Berlin (1960).\n\nAnd when Dylan Crossman was asked about his new work, \u201c[Insert Title],\u201d in a postshow talk, he wryly admitted that it had been a trio until he added a central role for himself at one week\u2019s notice, and that the music had arrived the day before the opening.\n\nAll three of these dances opened last week; all had glorious dancing. Mr. Gotheiner\u2019s 60-minute work ran at the Brooklyn Academy of Music\u2019s BAM Fisher space from Wednesday to Saturday, while between Thursday and Saturday, Mr. Crossman and Mr. Teicher shared a split-bill program, curated by David Parker, at Gibney Dance Studios.\n\nMr. Gotheiner\u2019s audacity is the most obvious. How do you turn any literary work into dance without failing right, left and center? Anyone who knows Kerouac\u2019s novel could watch this dance with a list of \u201cbuts\u201d accumulating in her head. Mr. Gotheiner would have spared himself trouble if he\u2019d chosen a different title.\n\nYet he made a good \u2014 often wonderful \u2014 dance. With a different title, he might still have evoked aspects of Kerouac while, in a good way, calling much else to mind. He introduced the \u201croad\u201d idea with unpeopled film, landscape hurtling past as if seen from a rushing car. Then, he gave us a two-tier dance, with dance onscreen (set in various landscapes) coexisting with dance onstage; the same dancers appeared in both. His chief subject is anthropological: the new American society of the Beat age, informal in manners and sexual suggestiveness. (At times Mr. Gotheiner labored too hard to make sure we noticed the bisexual currents amid his men.)\n\nHis dancers \u2014 his group is ZviDance \u2014 were vividly individual, marvelous (the men, especially) in showing two opposite physical impulses at the same time. I can\u2019t forget a solo in which one man, his back to us, danced as if his right shoulder and left foot were following different drummers, as if possessed. And Mr. Gotheiner caught the road theme in the ways dances passed across the stage with changing characters, and in the kinesthetically appealing combination of intense vitality and casual manners.\n\nChoreographers display no less courage (arrogance?) when they tackle great music \u2014 and, especially, great musicians in top form. There are hundreds of classic Fitzgerald recordings, but she\u2019s especially, and happily, aflame in the Rome and Berlin concerts. In one of the Berlin performance\u2019s extended cadenzas, laden with scat, she quotes highlights from her past as if this were an operatic mad scene.\n\nThere\u2019s no dancing version of Ms. Fitzgerald in Mr. Teicher\u2019s \u201cMeet Ella.\u201d This is a long male duet, in which he and Nathan Bugh, physically and stylistically unalike, dance together in soft-shoe tap style. (Though you can hear them, this tap dancing is not really for the ear.) Mr. Bugh, head shaven, almost always dances with an innocently happy smile but without looking the audience in the eye; the more youthful Mr. Teicher, with dark hair (and one white streak), is skillful and sly in the communicative use of his eyes and hands. The slyness never annoys; nothing here is overcalculated.\n\nEach man has solos, but there\u2019s also plenty of same-sex partnering, with lifts and supported turns. The meters and body language keep changing. No, they\u2019re not on her level, and yet they help you listen more closely to her.\n\nThe independence of dance from music in Mr. Crossman\u2019s work has been widespread since Merce Cunningham and John Cage established the separate coexistence of the two in the 1950s; Mr. Crossman danced with the Cunningham troupe from 2009 to 2011. Soon, images flew by like shooting stars: a spinning world that looked like a blue-green chessboard; himself on a stretcher in front of a hospital; his parents, gazing at him with aching sadness as he reached out to them, suffused with childlike love.\n\nPsilocybin has been illegal in the United States for more than 40 years. But Mr. Mihai, who had just finished treatment for Stage 3 Hodgkin\u2019s lymphoma, was participating in a study looking at whether the drug can reduce anxiety and depression in cancer patients. Throughout that eight-hour session, a psychiatrist and a social worker from NYU Langone Medical Center stayed by his side.\n\nPublished Thursday, the results from that study, and a similar small, controlled trial, were striking. Side effects were minimal.\n\nIn both trials, the intensity of the mystical experience described by patients correlated with the degree to which their depression and anxiety decreased.\n\nThe studies, by researchers at New York University, with 29 patients, and at Johns Hopkins University, with 51, were released concurrently in The Journal of Psychopharmacology. But by 1970, when those drugs were placed in the most restricted regulatory category, research ground to a near halt.\n\nSince about 2000, investigators have begun studying them, mostly with private funding. These two studies built on a 2011 UCLA psilocybin pilot project with 12 cancer patients.\n\nBoth share similarities. All volunteers had diagnoses of cancer-related anxiety or depression. Within seven weeks, they were given the other sample.\n\nAll patients were educated about the drug, monitored by two people throughout the placebo and psilocybin sessions, and seen for follow-up evaluation.\n\nResearchers created seven-hour music playlists, paced to the anticipated rhythms of the drug reaction. Against a backdrop of pastel colors and an up-tempo beat, a diverse cast of athletes testified:\n\n\u201cI meditate to crush it.\u201d\n\n\u201cI meditate to not freak out.\u201d\n\n\u201cI meditate to have the edge.\u201d\n\nThe ad was for Headspace, a guided meditation app developed by two Britons \u2014 a former monk and an advertising executive. By targeting the vast audience of football fans, the Headspace team was making clear its belief that meditation is something with mass commercial appeal.\n\nTheir pitch has new resonance on the heels of a bitter election. In the week after Donald J. Trump\u2019s victory in the presidential race, Headspace said it had experienced a 44 percent jump in its app\u2019s \u201cSOS\u201d meditation, a sort of panic button for the frazzled. Yet even without an anxious electorate, Headspace was finding an audience.\n\nA standout among a group of websites, apps and consulting firms that are seeking to capitalize on the current craze for all things mindful, the Headspace app has been downloaded more than 11 million times.\n\nWith its coffers full from a recent funding round befitting a Silicon Valley social media darling, Headspace is now aiming to convert millions more people with advertisements on network television and posters plastered on New York City bus stops and subway stations.\n\nYet even as Headspace finds its groove, questions linger about just how broad its appeal can be, and whether the app\u2019s creators can back up their aspirational claims with real science.\n\n\u201cThe cheerleading phase is over,\u201d said James Gimian, publisher of Mindful magazine. \u201cPeople know mindfulness can be a good thing. Now people need to do research into what the benefits are in specific applications.\u201d\n\nHeadspace was founded by Rich Pierson and his buddy Andy Puddicombe in 2010. At the time, Mr. Pierson was a stressed-out ad executive in London, and Mr. Puddicombe \u2014 who had studied as a Buddhist monk and dabbled as a circus performer \u2014 was teaching meditation to anxious Britons.\n\nThe two men agreed to barter: Mr. Puddicombe would teach Mr. Pierson how to meditate in exchange for help in developing something with mass market potential.\n\nBy 2012, they had their first app. Mr. Puddicombe, with his chummy British accent, honed and narrated the teachings, which were delivered through a mix of cartoons, videos of Mr. Puddicombe and podcasts. And the app gained decent traction, for a meditation app at least.\n\nBut in 2013, the pair moved the company from London to Los Angeles. England, Mr. Pierson said, is \u201cone of the most cynical markets in the world,\u201d and the co-founders were optimists. Also, they wanted to surf.\n\nIn 2014 they updated the app. Before that relaunch, they had fewer than one million downloads. Since then, growth has taken off. Last year Headspace raised $30 million in a financing round led by the Chernin Group and rounded out by celebrities including Jared Leto and Jessica Alba.\n\nToday, Headspace claims to employ 160 people at offices in Los Angeles, London and San Francisco. The company doesn\u2019t disclose revenue.\n\nWhat Headspace is selling is deceptively simple. By instructing people to focus on their breathing and let go of thoughts and emotions, Mr. Puddicombe gently coaxes users back to fuller engagement with the present moment. In modern parlance, it is mindfulness \u2014 a quick, secularized adaptation of Buddhist teachings that have been distilled for a modern, Western audience. A 10-day course on the app is free. Annual subscriptions cost about $100.\n\nFor this price, the goal is not so much enlightenment as it is stress relief and self-care.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s great if people think it can help them with sleep and productivity and the rest, but really what we\u2019re trying to do is get them to be more compassionate,\u201d Mr. Pierson said. \u201cAnd wouldn\u2019t that be an amazing skill for the world to learn at the moment?\u201d\n\nOf course, compassion wasn\u2019t what Headspace was pitching in the commercials that aired during the football game. And therein lies one of the inherent tensions with a for-profit meditation company: In the rush to expand a business, it\u2019s easy to misrepresent, oversell or exaggerate the benefits of a product, especially something as mercurial as mindfulness.\n\nIn particular, some claim that Headspace has gone too far in trying to appeal to those in fragile emotional states by offering meditation courses devised to treat depression.\n\n\u201cThe problem I have with Headspace is the way they\u2019ve expanded their content to target vulnerable populations,\u201d said Zindel Segal, a professor of psychology at the University of Toronto, and the co-developer of Mindfulness Based Cognitive Therapy, a meditation-based treatment for depression.\n\n\u201cThey have no research that validates the claims they are making in this area,\u201d Professor Segal said, \u201cyet their business model requires that they constantly grow their user base.\u201d\n\nHeadspace says it is sensitive to these concerns.\n\n\u201cScience is one of those things we take really seriously,\u201d Mr. Pierson said. Headspace has dozens of studies going on, employs many scientists who are building on the already voluminous body of research documenting meditation\u2019s potential benefits, and says it will publish more research soon.\n\nMr. Pierson also noted that Headspace tells users to seek professional help if they have severe depression and said, \u201cThis isn\u2019t meant to replace medication.\u201d\n\nYet in the end, Mr. Pierson was somewhat defiant.\n\n\u201cWe are just trying to put stuff out there that will help people,\u201d he said. The findings appeared in the Journal of the American Heart Association.\n\nCompared with the 1995-99 period, the rate of stroke in 2010-14 increased by 147 percent in people 35 to 39, by 101 percent in people 40 to 44, by 68 percent in those 45 to 49, and by 23 percent in the 50 to 54 group.\n\nStroke is still far more common in older people. But the rate decreased by 11 percent in those 55 to 59, by 22 percent in the 60 to 64 group, and by 18 percent in people 65 to 69.\n\nThe reasons are unclear, but the lead author, Joel N. Swerdel, now an epidemiologist with Janssen Pharmaceuticals, said that increasing obesity and diabetes in younger people are probably involved.\n\n\u201cFor a person 30 to 50, the good news is you ain\u2019t dead yet,\u201d he said. \u201cWith behavioral changes, changing diet, increasing exercise, there\u2019s still hope for you. For 12 years Cassini has been buzzing about Saturn, its rings and its moons. As a result we know that there are methane lakes on Titan and jets of water shooting from Enceladus, and the rings themselves have warps, ripples, hills twists and braids.\n\nNow the Cassini spacecraft has gradually shifted into an orbit that takes it over the planet\u2019s north and south poles and then down into a series of increasingly vertiginous-looking dives perpendicular to the plane of its buttery glowing rings.\n\nStarting on Wednesday, as shown here, with a gravitational nudge from the moon Titan, Cassini is set to commence a series of 20 dives just outside the outer edge of the main ring system. Along the way the spacecraft will try to sample ring particles and gases that live there in its vicinity, and pass only 56,000 miles above Saturn\u2019s cloud tops.\n\nIn April, Cassini will shift its orbit again to slice between the planet and the rings, sneaking as close as 1,012 miles from the clouds of Saturn in 22 more orbits. And it is just one of several disputes between universities and student newspapers, which are pushing administrations to become more transparent about sexual assault, a defining issue on campuses around the country.\n\nWith cuts at traditional news organizations, student journalists see their role as increasingly important in shedding light on the subject and are becoming more dogged in ferreting out information about sexual assault cases, particularly when faculty or student perpetrators could simply find other jobs or transfer to another university. Some are demanding that the student body be given details when a college confirms wrongdoing, particularly of a violent nature, by students, faculty or staff members.\n\nUniversities, though, often invoke privacy concerns in refusing to make details of inquiries public.\n\n\u201cThe critical question is whether we are able to continue protecting the confidentiality and privacy of victim-survivors who courageously come forward to report details of their victimization,\u201d wrote the University of Kentucky\u2019s president, Eli Capilouto, in a universitywide email.\n\n\u201cThe protection of victim-survivor privacy,\u201d the email continued, \u201crequires more than the redaction of names. You can rebuild them, even if you are middle-aged or older. \n\n\u201cOur lab and others have shown repeatedly\u201d that older muscles will grow and strengthen, says Marcas Bamman, a professor of integrative biology at the University of Alabama at Birmingham. In his studies, men and women in their 60s and 70s who began supervised weight training developed muscles that were as large and strong as those of your average 40-year-old.\n\nBut the process of bulking up works differently in older people than in the young, he said. \n\nSkeletal muscles are composed of various types of fibers and \u201ctwo things happen\u201d to those fibers after we reach middle age, Dr. Bamman says. Some die, especially if we have not been exercising our muscles much. Sedentary adults can lose 30 to 40 percent of the total number of fibers in their muscles by the time they are 55, Dr. Bamman says. \n\nOthers of the fibers remain alive but shrink and atrophy as we age.\n\nYoung people who work out add new muscle fibers and also plump up their existing ones. Older people do not. We increase the size of our atrophied muscle fibers with exercise but, for a variety of physiological reasons, do not add to the number of fibers, Dr. Bamman says. \n\nBut in practical terms, who cares? Older muscles will become larger and stronger if you work them, Dr. Bamman says. \n\nThe key, he continues, is regular and progressive weight training. If you don\u2019t belong to a gym, consider joining one, and then plan on tiring yourself. In order to initiate the biochemical processes that lead to larger, stronger fibers, Dr. Bamman says, you should push your muscles until they are exhausted. \n\nIn his studies, volunteers used weights calibrated so that the lifters could barely complete a set of eight to 12 repetitions before their arms or legs grew leaden and they had to rest. They repeated each set two or three times and visited the gym three times per week. If you are new to weight workouts, ask for an orientation at your gym or consult an athletic trainer who often works with older clients. \n\nDo you have a health question?\n\t\t Submit your question to Ask Well.\n", "unforeseeable annual increased chain web food year arctic global percent results ocean production base scientists estimated alters warming greening": "\nThe Arctic Ocean may seem remote and forbidding, but to birds, whales and other animals, it\u2019s a top-notch dining destination.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s a great place to get food in the summertime, so animals are flying or swimming thousands of miles to get there,\u201d said Kevin R. Arrigo, a biological oceanographer at Stanford University.\n\nBut the menu is changing. Confirming earlier research, scientists reported Wednesday that global warming is altering the ecology of the Arctic Ocean on a huge scale.\n\nThe annual production of algae, the base of the food web, increased an estimated 47 percent between 1997 and 2015, and the ocean is greening up much earlier each year.\n\nThese changes are likely to have a profound impact for animals further up the food chain, such as birds, seals, polar bears and whales. But scientists still don\u2019t know enough about the biology of the Arctic Ocean to predict what the ecosystem will look like in decades to come.\n\nWhile global warming has affected the whole planet in recent decades, nowhere has been hit harder than the Arctic. This month, temperatures in the high Arctic have been as much as 36 degrees above average, according to records kept by the Danish Meteorological Institute.\n\nIn October, the extent of sea ice was 28.5 percent below average \u2014 the lowest for the month since scientists began keeping records in 1979. The area of missing ice is the size of Alaska and Texas put together.\n\nSince the mid-2000s, researchers like Dr. Arrigo have been trying to assess the effects of retreating ice on the Arctic ecosystem.\n\nThe sun returns to the Arctic each spring and melts some of the ice that formed in winter. Algae in the open water quickly spring to life and start growing.\n\nThese algae are the base of the food chain in the Arctic Ocean, grazed by krill and other invertebrates that in turn support bigger fish, mammals and birds.\n\nDr. Arrigo and his colleagues visited the Arctic in research ships to examine algae in the water and to determine how it affected the water\u2019s color. They then reviewed satellite images of the Arctic Ocean, relying on the color of the water to estimate how much algae was growing \u2014 what scientists call the ocean\u2019s productivity.\n\nThe sea\u2019s productivity was rapidly increasing, Dr. Arrigo found. Last year he and his colleagues published their latest update, estimating that the productivity of the Arctic rose 30 percent between 1998 and 2012.\n\nBut Mati Kahru, an oceanographer at the University of California, San Diego, was skeptical. As an expert on remote sensing, he knew how hard it is to get a reliable picture of the Arctic Ocean.\n\nThe ocean is notoriously cloudy, and algae are not the only thing that tinting the water. Rivers deliver tea-colored organic matter into the Arctic Ocean, which can give the impression that there\u2019s more algae in the water than is actually there.\n\nDr. Kahru and his colleagues decided to take an independent look, scouring satellite databases for images taken from 1997 to 2015 \u2014 \u201cevery image available,\u201d he said.\n\nThe scientists used a mathematical equation to determine how the color in each pixel of each image was determined by algae, runoff, and other factors. Dr. Kahru decided that Dr. Arrigo was right: The Arctic Ocean has become vastly more productive.\n\nMarcel Babin, an oceanographer at Universit\u00e9 Laval in Quebec who was not involved in the new study, said that the researchers had done \u201cvery careful work\u201d that confirmed the earlier studies. \u201cIt\u2019s an important finding,\u201d he said.\n\nNot only is the Arctic Ocean producing more algae, but it\u2019s doing so sooner each year. \u201cThese blooms are coming earlier, sometimes two months earlier,\u201d Dr. Kahru said.\n\nIn fact, the bloom may be coming even sooner than satellites can record. On research cruises, Dr. Arrigo and his colleagues have found that open water is no longer a requirement for algae to grow.\n\nThe ice has gotten so thin that sunlight reaches through it. \u201cNow they\u2019re not even waiting for the ice to melt,\u201d said Dr. Arrigo said of algal organisms.\n\nIf we stay on our current course, pouring more carbon dioxide into the atmosphere, the Arctic will only get warmer, perhaps becoming ice-free in the summer. If algae can find more nitrogen and other nutrients in the ocean, its productivity may continue to rise.\n\nScientists can\u2019t yet say what the ecological effects of this transformation will be. \u201cIt is probable it will have an impact on the whole food web,\u201d Dr. Babin said.\n\nDr. Babin and his colleagues have been studying that impact over the past two summers on an expedition called the Green Edge Project, which has studied the ecology in Baffin Bay off the coast of northern Canada. They hope to present the first results of the survey next year.\n\nSome species may thrive because they can graze on the extra algae. But if the ecosystem comes to life earlier in the year, many species may be left behind.\n\nFish larvae may not be able to develop fast enough. Migrating whales and birds may show up too late. A lot of the extra algae may drop to the sea floor by then, untouched.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s going to be a different Arctic unless we turn things around,\u201d said Dr. Arrigo.\n", "summer supreme burial requiring focus past facilities cremation court perform pay state front texas shifted seeking abortions losing case places obstacles remains fetal": "\nMIAMI \u2014 Despite losing a milestone abortion case at the United States Supreme Court this past summer, Texas threw down another stumbling block this week.
