The pain started a day or two later as a blistering rash swept down from her scalp onto her forehead. \u201cMy eye was so swollen I couldn\u2019t open it,\u201d she said.\n\nShingles. Ms. Abate, 76, a retired casino supervisor in Rio Rancho, N.M., had had the disease twice before, in her 60s, but the episode three years ago hit particularly hard.\n\nLong after the rash healed, which took about three weeks, she suffered the complication called postherpetic neuralgia \u2014 lingering nerve pain that can last for months or even, as in her case, years. \u201cI get up every day, and it\u2019s there,\u201d she said. \u201cI go to sleep, and it\u2019s there.\u201d\n\nShe had heard something about a shingles vaccine, but \u201cI really didn\u2019t pay attention,\u201d Ms. Abate confessed. And she is hardly unusual.\n\nIt\u2019s an ongoing and vexing public health problem: People once vigilant about vaccinating their children aren\u2019t nearly as careful about protecting themselves as they age, even though diseases like influenza, pneumonia and shingles (a.k.a. herpes zoster) are particularly dangerous for older people.\n\n\u201cTrying to prevent these common and often debilitating conditions is incredibly important for older adults,\u201d said Dr.Carolyn Bridges, associate director for adult immunization at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. Yet in the C.D.C.\u2019s 2014 and 2015 reports on vaccination coverage, she said, \u201cwe really didn\u2019t see much change.\u201d\n\nMost Americans over 65 get an annual flu shot, but the proportion actually declined a few percentage points last season to about 63 percent. The C.D.C. estimates that of the 226,000 people hospitalized for flu in an average year, 50 percent to 70 percent are over 65; so are the great majority of those who die from it. \u201cOlder adults take the brunt,\u201d Dr. Bridges said.\n\nSimilarly, in 2014, about 61 percent of older adults had received one or both of the two pneumococcal vaccines, which protect against pneumococcal infections that can lead to pneumonia and meningitis. That represented no improvement, leaving millions of older people still vulnerable.\n\nAbout 58 percent of older people had been vaccinated against tetanus during the past 10 years, but only 14 percent had received the recommended dose of the Tdap vaccine against tetanus, diphtheria and whooping cough. It\u2019s especially important for grandparents and others who have contact with infants too young to be vaccinated.\n\nAnd elders have been particularly slow to take advantage of the shingles vaccine. The Food and Drug Administration approved it a decade ago, and the C.D.C. recommends it for those over 60, including those who\u2019ve already had shingles.\n\nCoverage has climbed steadily, but in 2014 had still reached only 31 percent of those over 65. As with nearly all of these vaccines, older whites were more likely to have been vaccinated than blacks, Hispanics or Asians.\n\nWhy these missed opportunities?\n\n\u201cVaccines are less likely to be routinely incorporated in adult medical practice,\u201d Dr. Bridges said. \u201cEvery time a child comes in, a pediatrician makes sure they\u2019re up-to-date.\u201d\n\nOlder adults often have medical issues that take precedence during brief office visits. They also see specialists who are more focused on cardiology or oncology than on flu and shingles.\n\nSeniors and their caregivers should request vaccinations; the C.D.C. publishes guidelines and a quiz that explain which ones are recommended. Zostavax, the current shingles vaccine, reduces the risk of the disease in adults over 60 by half, and the incidence of postherpetic neuralgia by two-thirds.\n\n(Quick primer: Shingles results from the same virus that causes chickenpox, which nearly all older Americans have had. The virus typically remains dormant for decades, but the odds of its reactivation rise steeply after 50 as the immune system weakens. The lifetime risk of shingles is one in three, rising to one in two for those over 85.)\n\nThe vaccine\u2019s underuse can be blamed, in part, on supply shortages in its early years until about 2012. The manufacturer, Merck, and the C.D.C. didn\u2019t increase media campaigns until vaccine supplies were sufficient; such campaigns had just started when Ms. Abate became ill. It\u2019s not surprising that she was only vaguely aware of Zostavax.\n\n(She\u2019s starting to feel better, but still needs a sedative to sleep and Zoloft for the depression that chronic pain can produce. \u201cIt\u2019s been a long road,\u201d she said. Now vaccinated against a shingles recurrence, she\u2019s urging her children to get the shot.)\n\nCost remains a barrier to getting Zostavax and some other adult vaccines.\n\nIn a study published this past summer, researchers reported that nearly 40 percent of the time, patients over 50 who requested a prescription for Zostavax at a pharmacy chain chose not to receive the vaccine; out-of-pocket costs were most frequently the reason.\n\nThe Affordable Care Act requires private insurers to cover Zostavax without co-pays for people older than 60, and many cover it for policyholders over 50. But Medicare beneficiaries find that, unlike the flu and pneumococcal vaccines, which are covered under Part B and often administered in physicians\u2019 offices, Zostavax and Tdap are covered under Part D.\n\nPhysicians can\u2019t easily bill for Part D reimbursement, so they often send patients to pharmacies, which can. But because Part D involves a welter of different plans and formularies, some requiring patients to pay for the vaccine and then seek reimbursement, the cost and co-pays can discourage use. Zostavax, at about $200 a dose, is the most expensive adult vaccine.\n\nThis landscape could change drastically in a year or so. In October, the pharmaceutical giant GlaxoSmithKline submitted a new shingles vaccine for F.D.A. approval.\n\nInternational studies indicate that the newcomer, Shingrix, is far more effective than the current vaccine, reducing the incidence of shingles by 90 percent. Moreover, the effectiveness doesn\u2019t appear to decrease among older age groups, as Zostavax\u2019s does.\n\nShingrix has its own drawbacks. For one, it requires a second injected dose several months after the first; some people won\u2019t follow up. The manufacturer has yet to set a price, and unless Congress changes the law, any new vaccine will face the same Part D billing complications.\n\nBut if the F.D.A. approves it, and the C.D.C. recommends its use (which triggers insurance coverage), Shingrix may also prevent a lot of shingles cases \u2014 but only if older adults are actually vaccinated.\n\nThey don\u2019t have a great track record.\n\nPublic health leaders don\u2019t want older adults waiting to see what the F.D.A. does. One million Americans will get shingles in the coming year; the C.D.C. wants seniors to get vaccinated now.\n\nDr. Bridges endorsed a unfestive but pragmatic holiday gift idea I suggested: a card with a promise to take an older family member to get vaccinated, and to pay any charge or co-pay.\n\nDr. Bridges suggested another gift: that younger family members get flu shots themselves. But shortly after the upper stage separated from the core booster, flight telemetry stopped, leaving ground controllers confused about what had happened.\n\nRoscosmos later confirmed that the Progress 65 and its cargo had been destroyed, with the failure occurring about 120 miles over Tuva, a Russian republic in southern Siberia.\n\nThe six astronauts at the space station \u2014 three Russian, two American and one French \u2014 are safe and have adequate supplies, Roscosmos and NASA said.\n\nThe cargo included 1,400 pounds of propellant, 112 pounds of oxygen, 925 pounds of water, and 2,750 pounds of spare parts, supplies and scientific experiment hardware. Their average age was 72. They gave them eye examinations, took blood samples and interviewed them about their health and behavior over the years, estimating exposure to ultraviolet B rays.\n\nThe study, in JAMA Ophthalmology, found that those with the highest UVB exposure, especially in the teenage and young adult years, had about a 30 percent lower risk for myopia than those with the lowest exposure. Exposure to sunlight increases vitamin D levels, but vitamin D levels did not correlate with nearsightedness.\n\nThey also found that more schooling increased the risk of nearsightedness. What happened next is uncertain, but Ms. Phiri has heard that a third poacher, unknown to her mother, was hiding in the bush. He jumped out and slashed her colleague\u2019s head with a machete.\n\nMs. Paundi, who was unarmed, ran. But the poachers gave chase, and when they caught her, they killed her.\n\nMs. Paundi, 38, was the breadwinner for her five siblings, and she left behind five children, now orphans. Though she died serving her country and protecting its wildlife, she had no life insurance, and officials offered no assistance to her family. \u201cThey didn\u2019t even say anything to us,\u201d Ms. Phiri said.\n\nThe world depends on individuals like Ms. Paundi to protect increasingly imperiled wildlife. But many rangers do not receive the support they need. A recent World Wildlife Fund study of 570 rangers in 12 African countries found that 59 percent did not have basic supplies like boots, tents and GPS devices, and that 42 percent had not received adequate training.\n\nDespite the critical role rangers play in the poaching crisis, conservation organizations tend to overlook the need for everyday resources, said Peter Newland, the director of training at 51 Degrees, a private security company in Kenya.\n\n\u201cDonors outside of Africa want to see sexy, high-tech solutions like drones and ground sensors, not to hear about the need for warm clothing, boots and better food for rangers,\u201d he said. \u201cLarge nongovernmental groups spend huge amounts, yet there are rangers calling me for socks.\u201d\n\nThe wildlife fund study also found that 82 percent of rangers had faced life-threatening situations, including attacks by poachers and animals. Ms. Paundi was one of more than 1,000 rangers killed on the job over the past 10 years, according to the Thin Green Line Foundation, which supports park rangers and their families. Many receive little or nothing from the government.\n\nAnother World Wildlife Fund study, in 10 African countries, found that just 60 percent of rangers had health insurance, 50 percent had life insurance and 40 percent had long-term disability insurance.\n\n\u201cImagine how demotivating it would be to see a mate killed and then to witness his family removed from their house and his kids taken out of school because they receive no wage as a thank you for his sacrifice after he\u2019s gone,\u201d said Sean Willmore, who founded the Thin Green Line Foundation. \u201cMorale plays a massive role in reducing poaching.\u201d\n\nGovernmental corruption makes a ranger\u2019s job even more difficult in many parts of Africa. Poachers have been supported by officials in a number of countries. But rangers would be in trouble even if there were no corruption, Mr. Willmore said.\n\nIn the last two years, the foundation has spent $1.2 million on equipment and training for rangers and has supported more than 150 families of those killed in the line of duty, including Ms. Paundi\u2019s. The organization is putting her children through school.\n\nSimilarly, For Rangers, a charity co-founded by Mr. Newland that raises money through extreme racing events, has donated $200,000 worth of gear, vehicles, medical supplies and family aid to rangers at 10 parks throughout Africa since April 2014.\n\n\u201cThere\u2019s a huge amount of recognition for the illegal wildlife trade crisis, but not a huge amount for the people really doing the day-to-day work to stop it,\u201d said Sam Taylor, a co-founder of For Rangers and the head of conservation projects at 51 Degrees.\n\nBut small nonprofit groups can support only a fraction of the estimated 20,000 to 25,000 rangers working in Africa.\n\n\u201cPerformance is definitely affected by the lack of insurance, combined with the high risk factor and the lack of training and equipment,\u201d said Rohit Singh, the lead author of the World Wildlife Fund studies. \u201cHow can we expect rangers to deliver if they do not have these basic things?\u201d\n\nWhen rangers are well taken care of and receive appropriate training, poaching rates tend to drop, Mr. Singh and Mr. Willmore said.\n\nLewa Wildlife Conservancy, a wildlife sanctuary in Kenya, for example, was losing unprecedented numbers of rhinoceroses when it overhauled its operations in 2014. To turn things around, the conservancy invested in specialized training, brought in a helicopter, installed a new communications system and strengthened relationships with local communities.\n\nSince then, Lewa has not lost a single rhino.\n\n\u201cThere was a time when rhinos were poached here so often that we were becoming scared, but we\u2019ve tried our best and we\u2019ve stopped it,\u201d said Francis Kobia Chokera, 44, a ranger at Lewa. \u201cWe had security before, but not like it is now. It\u2019s very, very tight.\u201d\n\nMr. Chokera\u2019s job is still demanding: He patrols on foot for 12 hours a day, and like 47 percent of the rangers interviewed by the wildlife fund, he sees his family fewer than five days per month. But he has insurance, earns around $3,600 a year \u2014 more than two times the average income in Kenya \u2014 and receives overtime, free housing and a pension.\n\n\u201cBy good luck, I was given the chance to work here,\u201d he said. \u201cI\u2019ve always loved animals.\u201d\n\nTimothy Tear, executive director for the Wildlife Conservation Society\u2019s Africa program, said that success stories like Lewa\u2019s are \u201cwhere hope is found.\u201d He noted that the conservancy\u2019s considerable investment in security and ranger training \u2014 $1 million a year \u2014 has been integral to its success.\n\n\u201cIf you do the simple math of dollars spent to acres protected, you will find Lewa at the top of the investment-per-acre gradient,\u201d Dr. Tear said.\n\nOther protected areas that have received large investments in ranger training and support \u2014 like the Sabi Sand Game Reserve in South Africa \u2014 have seen similar gains against poachers. In 2013, the reserve lost 51 rhinos, but only two have been killed this year.\n\n\u201cOur rangers were herders, but now they\u2019re effectively soldiers,\u201d said David Powrie, Sabi Sand\u2019s warden. \u201cThey\u2019re at the center of all our operations.\u201d\n\nFor now, such cases are the exception, but Mr. Willmore said better and broader training could be a first step toward improving working conditions for rangers across Africa. The Thin Green Line Foundation plans to teach 30 to 50 exceptional rangers the skills needed to be trainers themselves, and then host six events a year at which they would instruct their colleagues.\n\n\u201cAt the moment, most training rangers get, if any, is expensive and done by expats, and there\u2019s no follow-up,\u201d Mr. Willmore said. \u201cBy training the trainers, we will potentially reach over 15,000 rangers in the next five years and help change the game on the ground.\u201d\n\nThough training would not solve problems like a lack of insurance and equipment, it could save the lives of both animals and rangers. Recently, a ranger who had been taught self-defense was attacked by a poacher with a machete and was able to use a stick to knock down the suspect and arrest him, said Craig Millar, the head of security for Kenya\u2019s Big Life Foundation, a community conservation group.\n\nMs. Paundi, the ranger in Zambia, had no such training. \u201cProtecting wildlife is good, but my mom didn\u2019t have enough protection herself,\u201d said Ms. Phiri, her daughter. \u201cI believe if my mother had the proper training, equipment and security, she would still be alive today.\u201d\n", "advice savage djs writer love complexities dan modern story adoptions dark reads columnist homeless weeks podcast open mommy": "\nIn this week\u2019s podcast, the author and advice columnist Dan Savage reads \u201cDJ\u2019s Homeless Mommy,\u201d his 2005 essay about the baggage that can come with an open adoption.\n\nYou can listen to his reading using the player above, or find the episode on iTunes and Google Play Music.\n\nMr. Savage is the creator of the syndicated advice column Savage Love, which focuses on sex and relationships. He\u2019s also the host of the weekly podcast Savage Lovecast. You can follow him on Twitter.\n", "heavy heart equator rolled plutos feature pluto facing heartshape bright coincidence largest moon side": "\nThe heart of Pluto is forever facing away from its largest moon, Charon.\n\nTo explain why is not a story of spurned love, although it does involve a heavy heart.\n\nTwo studies published on Wednesday by the journal Nature show how the surface of Pluto might have been scarred long ago when a smaller body slammed into it. The resulting crater then partially filled with denser nitrogen ice and possibly a bulge of liquid water from an underground ocean, and the additional mass caused Pluto to roll over, perhaps as much as 60 degrees, to its present configuration, the scientists conclude.\n\n\u201cThese are interesting and plausible papers,\u201d said David J. Stevenson, a professor of planetary science at the California Institute of Technology who was not involved with either paper.\n\nThe matter is simply gravity.\n\n\u201cIf you take Pluto and you put an extra weight on the surface of Pluto, then Pluto will roll over so that weight moves toward the equator and toward either the point directly underneath Charon or the point opposite that,\u201d said Francis Nimmo, a professor of earth and planetary sciences at the University of California, Santa Cruz, and lead author of one of the Nature papers.\n\nDr. Nimmo argues that the rolling over of Pluto is additional evidence for an ocean of liquid water beneath the icy crust.\n\nThe other researchers, led by James T. Keane, a graduate student at the University of Arizona, mapped networks of cracks on the surface that they say are the places where Pluto ruptured as a result of tectonic forces as it rolled over.\n\n\u201cImagine you have an egg, and you want to move that bulge to another part of the egg,\u201d Mr. Keane said. \u201cYou have to squish it, and that\u2019s going to cause it to break.\u201d\n\nAs Pluto reoriented, its spin axis largely remained pointing in the same direction. From the perspective of someone on the surface, it would have seemed that the location of the north pole was changing, in what astronomers call \u201ctrue polar wander.\u201d\n\nThe starting point of the research for both teams was the revelation of a bright splotch on the surface of Pluto just north of the equator in the shape of a Valentine heart, in images captured during the flyby of NASA\u2019s New Horizons spacecraft last year. Mission scientists named the splotch Tombaugh Regio after Clyde Tombaugh, who discovered Pluto. (\u201cRegio\u201d is the Latin word for \u201cregion.\u201d)\n\nPluto and Charon today are in what astronomers call a mutually tidally locked orbit of 6.4 days, the same side of Charon always facing Pluto, and the same side of Pluto facing Charon.\n\nAstronomers including Dr. Nimmo and Mr. Keane noticed that the left side of the bright heart, named Sputnik Planitia, is almost exactly on the side opposite from Charon, and it did not seem likely that it ended up there by chance.\n\n\u201cSputnik Planitia is located very close to that special point,\u201d Dr. Nimmo said, \u201cand so that makes you suspect Sputnik Planitia represents an extra weight.\u201d\n\nThe left lobe is elliptical, a 600-mile-wide depression that looks like a crater that was carved by an ancient impact. At first glance, that would seem to undercut the argument. \u201cSputnik Planitia is a hole in the ground,\u201d Dr. Nimmo said, \u201cso it shouldn\u2019t be excess mass. It should have less mass.\u201d\n\nFor this idea to work, the scientists had to explain how to make a hole in the ground be heavier, not lighter. If Sputnik Planitia had less mass, it should have rotated toward one of the poles instead.\n\nDr. Nimmo and his colleagues argue that water from an underground ocean welled upward, adding mass, because liquid water is denser than frozen water. (With Pluto\u2019s frigid temperatures, the water would have to be loaded with antifreeze \u2014 most likely ammonia \u2014 for it not to freeze.)\n\nNew Horizons detected a glacier of nitrogen ice, which is also denser than water ice, covering Sputnik Planitia.\n\nMr. Keane does not argue against an ocean, but he said a thick layer of nitrogen ice might be sufficient explanation.\n\nDouglas P. Hamilton, an astronomy professor at the University of Maryland, agrees that Sputnik Planitia is a heavy spot that caused Pluto to roll over, but he disagrees with the premise that it started as an impact crater.\n\nDr. Hamilton said he has a paper scheduled to run in the same print issue of Nature as the other two that explains how the basin could have formed simply from the weight of nitrogen ices pushing down on the crust. Because the paper has not yet been published online, he said he was prohibited by Nature\u2019s embargo policy from discussing details of his research.\n\nA spokeswoman for Nature said the journal\u2019s policy is to not talk about papers with anyone other than the authors until one week before publication.\n\nDr. Nimmo said Pluto\u2019s reorientation occurred quickly, within a few thousand years. Mr. Keane\u2019s model suggests that the change occurred more slowly, between one and 10 million years, to give the nitrogen ice enough time to accumulate in the basin.\n\nChecking the idea could prove impossible until planetary scientists are able to precisely measure Pluto\u2019s gravity. \u201cTo really test that, you need to put something in orbit around Pluto,\u201d Dr. Nimmo said, \u201cand that\u2019s not going to happen for a while.\u201d\n", "owning climate considered implications youre longterm idea damageprone buying home smart change areas": "\nSo you want to buy a home in a global warming zone.\n\nWait, you weren\u2019t thinking of it that way? You didn\u2019t even realize it or think to check? Well, it\u2019s time to adjust your outlook.\n\nThat was my conclusion, at least, after reading my colleague Ian Urbina\u2019s recent article about climate change and the residential real estate market. No one knows when (or if) a panic may set in among insurance companies, lenders or home buyers \u2014 one that causes prices to fall and never recover in vulnerable areas. But given that homes are the most expensive thing that many of us ever purchase, it\u2019s foolish not to consider the long-term implications of owning one in a growing number of increasingly damage-prone places.\n\nThis is also an area of financial life that is ripe for mistakes and delusional thinking. Buying a home involves an enormous amount of money, and few people do it often enough to be experts. Given the realities of climate change, the process is now set against a backdrop of radical uncertainty about the very ground you will live on and the air you will breathe. Throw political uncertainty into the mix and \u2014 well, good luck keeping your head on straight.\n\nGiven all that, you owe it to yourself to call on every dispassionate expert you can find and grab all available data on any risk you are taking on. Here is how to get started:\n\nTHE TEAM Yes, you will want a real estate professional working with you who has deep knowledge of the local market and has lived through a few floods, fires or hurricanes.\n\nBut that is just the start. Sasha Krigsvold moved to low-lying Norfolk, Va., in 2006, and nobody told her until she was at the closing table that her home would need a special flood insurance policy. A secretary went off into another room to start getting quotes. Ms. Krigsvold\u2019s advice? Establish a relationship with a local insurance agent before you start shopping for homes.\n\nNext up: A municipal flood expert, preferably someone from town or city government who can explain any and all regulations you might need to know about when or if you ever want or need to fix your place up.\n\nIt was only after a renovation, when inspectors were in her basement, that Ms. Krigsvold learned that everyone involved thus far had not realized that they had put a bunch of expensive mechanical equipment too far below where it needed to be to satisfy local flood-mitigation building codes. She would have to pull out everything and start over.\n\nOr would she? \u201cI cried,\u201d she recalled. \u201cMore men were called from downtown. I had 10 people walking around at one point as I stood bouncing my first baby on my hip, in tears. And they all decided to look the other way. But that would not happen today.\u201d\n\nTHE INSURANCE Homeowners insurance generally does not cover floods. Your lender may make you buy flood insurance, depending on where you live. The federal government runs the program, though for-profit insurance companies sell and administer the policies.\n\nEven if it is not a requirement, read every word of the Federal Emergency Management Agency\u2019s website on the flood insurance program before you buy a home. After nearly every big water-related weather event, whether it\u2019s in Louisiana, South Carolina or Texas, there are countless stories of people who did not have to buy flood insurance but wish that they had. FEMA\u2019s flood maps, which lenders use to figure out who to force to buy the coverage, are a good place to start. But keep in mind that they are backward-looking and do not account for global warming and its potential future impact.\n\nThe world of flood insurance is complicated \u2014 it can be expensive, and rules and maps sometimes change. All the more reason to shop as carefully for a local insurance agent as you would for a real estate professional, someone to whom you can pose any and every question you can think of \u2014 about the elevation certificate that governs the policy, grandfathering of old rates from previous owners, coverage limits and special policies that go beyond the standard amounts.\n\n\u201cYou should purchase the policy assuming that the loss will occur,\u201d said Christopher W. Heidrick, who runs an insurance agency in Sanibel, Fla. But you should also hope that it doesn\u2019t, given how much trouble some people have getting insurance companies to pay their claims in full after big storms.\n\nTHE DATA Want to know what sort of insurance claims the home has generated in the recent past? The information is available, but many home buyers don\u2019t know to ask for it.\n\nAt least two companies track this information, though you will generally need to either get the current homeowner to order a report or an insurance agent to do it for you. One report is called CLUE, for Comprehensive Loss Underwriting Exchange, and the other is called A-PLUS.\n\nAs with any such report, these may be wrong or inconclusive. Get both, follow up with the homeowner and ask about any flood insurance claims or FEMA grants that may not show up on the reports. Real estate disclosure rules in general range wildly from state to state, so just assume that you will have to ask for this or any other pertinent information about past damage. If a seller balks at any of it, that is a really bad sign.\n\nOne other site worth checking is homedisclosure.com, which offers a free report on weather and environmental risks as well as local demographic information and all sorts of scary stuff about the local sex offender population and any former meth labs.\n\nAlso on the home-buying checklist that Robert W. Tull Jr., a financial planner in Chesapeake, Va., gives to clients is an exhortation to make like a reporter and talk to any potential neighbors. There may be flooding events that have caused problems that other research won\u2019t turn up.\n\nTHE HOUSE TOUR Have a house in mind? First, check the features. Spencer M. Houldin, president of Ericson Insurance Advisors in Washington Depot, Conn., suggests looking out for special impact-resistant glass in the windows or hurricane shutters. \u201cSometimes, the solution may be as simple as precut plywood to screw on,\u201d he said.\n\nMr. Heidrick in Florida suggests a wind mitigation inspection, something that is standard in Florida but may not be elsewhere. Try to find a home inspector who can check how well the roof might hold up, in particular. He also recommends using the specs on the smarthomeamerica.org website as a guide.\n\nEllen Chamberlain, a Norfolk-based real-estate professional with Nancy Chandler Associates, looks for clues on her first pass through a basement, if a home has one. She can usually tell how much water has ever come in by how many cinder blocks high people have stored their belongings.\n\nOnce upstairs, a wholesale renovation, including new heating and cooling systems, is not always a good sign. \u201cBuyers from out of town get so excited about all that or the new hardwood floors,\u201d she said. \u201cBut I say \u2018Oh, wait a sec, it may be that the insurance company paid for all of that.\u2019\u201d And then she asks for a CLUE report to see about recent flooding.\n\nOutside, nearby trees can cause tens of thousands of dollars in damage if they fall. And in fire zones, you will need to learn about brush and all of the rules that come with it. Derek Ross, president of an insurance agency in Tarzana, Calif., said that people often forgot that much of his state was technically desert. And different insurance companies will make different demands about how much clearance a home needs from any brush that could catch fire.\n\nTHE MARKET All of the information you will gather from the tips so far is not predictive. Nothing is, especially with a president-elect who has said both that the Chinese invented global warming and also requested a sea wall to protect his Scottish golf course from it.\n\nBut fear in the mortgage industry is real. TD Bank was out pitching stories this week to random journalists with voluminous tips about buying a home, including an offer to speak to an expert. When I asked to speak to him about how or whether to buy in a global warming zone, however, the company went radio silent.\n\nIn those areas of the country, large numbers of people are staying away from any place where they might have to, say, pay for flood insurance. He was 93.\n\nHis nephew Roger Fox announced the death last Wednesday.\n\nMr. Moss was a nightclub comedian and master of ceremonies in the tradition of Henny Youngman. His comedy was based around well-trodden one-liners, bits and impersonations.\n\nHe opened for performers like the singer Robert Goulet and appeared on television with Milton Berle, Merv Griffin and Johnny Carson. He was also known as a master of the put-on, convincing unwitting audiences at official events that he was a legitimate speaker, like a chief executive or an ambassador, and then slowly veering from the reasonable into the absurd before revealing that he was a comedian.\n\nAnd he appeared in commercials, of which the most famous was the Alka-Seltzer spot. Written by Howie Cohen and directed by Bob Pasqualina, it featured Mr. Moss sitting on the side of his bed with a hangdog expression. He slowly shakes his head while repeating the line \u201cI can\u2019t believe I ate that whole thing.\u201d (The line is often remembered as \u201cI can\u2019t believe I ate the whole thing.\u201d)\n\nThe commercial struck a chord with dyspeptic viewers around the country, and in 1977 it was admitted to the advertising industry\u2019s Clio Awards Hall of Fame. The advertisement made Mr. Moss recognizable to a national audience, helping him book more conventions, club dates and TV appearances.\n\n\u201cThat commercial changed my whole life,\u201d Mr. Moss told the television historian Kliph Nesteroff in an interview in 2011.\n\nMr. Moss\u2019s most famous line has resurfaced in popular culture several times. For example, \u201cI can\u2019t believe I ate the whole thing\u201d was the quotation accompanying Homer Simpson\u2019s unkempt high school yearbook photo on an episode of \u201cThe Simpsons.\u201d\n\nMilton Moss was born in the Bronx on Oct. 25, 1922, to Anton Moss and the former Eva Goldstein. A new study suggests it wouldn\u2019t.\n\nResearchers at Cambridge University provided written information about the risk factors, prevention, treatment and consequences of Type 2 diabetes to 569 healthy middle-aged men and women. Then they randomly assigned them to one of three groups.\n\nOne group got only the written advice.\n\nThe second received a \u201cgenetic risk score\u201d based on methods used by genetic testing companies. The third received a score based on health characteristics like age, sex, smoking status, blood pressure and family history of diabetes. Both scores included a percentage estimate of lifetime risk as well as a verbal estimate of whether someone was at \u201cbelow average,\u201d \u201caverage\u201d or \u201cabove average\u201d risk.\n\nResearchers interviewed them eight weeks later and found no differences between the groups in what they ate or how active they were. The edition is a response to significant German interest in Charlie Hebdo after the attack, editors said this week.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s an experiment,\u201d G\u00e9rard Biard, Charlie Hebdo\u2019s editor in chief, said on Wednesday. \u201cIt\u2019s an answer to this particular interest, which is not only about what happened and freedom of expression but also about ideas.\u201d\n\nMr. Biard said the paper had been the subject of numerous exhibitions, awards and news coverage in Germany since the attack on Jan. 7, 2015, in which two brothers, Sa\u00efd and Ch\u00e9rif Kouachi, stormed the offices in the 11th Arrondissement of Paris with assault rifles, leaving 12 dead.\n\nWith an initial print run of 200,000, the new edition will be available across Germany every Thursday. The newspaper was targeted by jihadists after it published cartoons depicting the Prophet Muhammad, but it also regularly takes aim at the Vatican and the European political establishment.\n\nDetractors have accused Charlie Hebdo of heedlessly printing incendiary and racist imagery, while supporters see it as a symbol of free expression and Western liberal values.\n\nThe newspaper\u2019s \u201cSurvivors\u201d edition, published a week after the attack on its offices and once again depicting Muhammad, sold 70,000 copies in Germany, editors said.\n\nThe editor of the German edition, who uses the pseudonym Minka Schneider, said, \u201cGermans feel particularly close to France and to Charlie Hebdo, and the debate about freedom of expression is very passionate here compared to other countries.\u201d\n\nMs. Schneider, 33, who supervises a staff of 12, mostly translators in the Paris office, said that security was not the primary reason for concealing her real name. \u201cIt\u2019s really because I just want to focus on the journal, rather than fielding emails,\u201d she said.\n\nThe first German issue, with 16 pages, features a four-page travel feature by the cartoonist Laurent Sourisseau, who uses the pen name Riss, depicting people he met across Germany and their thoughts on cultural heritage, national identity and the influx of hundreds of thousands of refugees, most of them Muslims, in the last several years.\n\nThe reaction in the German news media has largely been positive, with a few exceptions. Trump has signaled an undiminished determination to repeal President Obama\u2019s signature domestic achievement, the Affordable Care Act, and replace it with a health law that would be far less comprehensive.\n\nAnd Mr. Trump is handing Republicans and their base voters what they have clamored for since the Affordable Care Act became law in 2010 \u2014 a powerful force to reverse course.\n\nRepublicans were pleased.\n\n\u201cI can tell you where we\u2019re going to start: with a process to repeal and replace Obamacare,\u201d Senator Mitch McConnell of Kentucky, the majority leader, said Tuesday.\n\nMr. Price, an orthopedic surgeon from Atlanta\u2019s affluent northern suburbs, was one of the first lawmakers to draft a full replacement for the Affordable Care Act. His proposal would take health care in a fundamentally different direction, away from mandated coverage and care and toward a free-market approach, with fewer consumer protections and more freedoms for doctors.\n\n\u201cThe president-elect has made it very clear: He wants the Congress, when they convene in early January, to take up the task of repealing and replacing Obamacare first,\u201d Vice President-elect Mike Pence said Tuesday on Fox News. He described Mr. Price as \u201csomeone who literally, for the last half a dozen years, has been in the forefront of efforts, not only to repeal Obamacare, but put forward common sense, free-market solutions that will lower the cost of health insurance, without growing the size of government.\u201d\n\nSome 20 million uninsured people have gained coverage under the Affordable Care Act, reducing the nation\u2019s uninsured rate to 8.6 percent, the lowest on record, the Obama administration says. The Congressional Budget Office has not estimated the cost of Mr. Price\u2019s legislation or its effects on coverage, but opponents say the human cost would be steep.\n\nThe Price bill, the Empowering Patients First Act, would weaken many protections for consumers in the Affordable Care Act. The law, for example, prohibits insurers from denying coverage or charging higher rates because of a person\u2019s pre-existing medical conditions. Under the bill, this type of protection would be available to people who have maintained coverage in the past \u2014 for example, people moving to the individual market from an employer\u2019s health plan.\n\nThe federal requirement for most Americans to have health insurance or pay a tax penalty would be eliminated, as would the requirement for larger employers to offer coverage. Billions of dollars that 31 states expect to receive for expanding Medicaid eligibility would also disappear.\n\nInstead, Mr. Price would offer tax credits for the purchase of individual and family health insurance policies. The credits would increase as people grow older and are likely to need more medical care. He would use other tax breaks to coax more people into contributing to health savings accounts that could be used to help pay medical expenses. Such accounts are championed by conservatives as a way to slow the growth of health costs.\n\nThe bill would make it easier for doctors to defend themselves against medical malpractice lawsuits. Patients would have a heavier burden of proof if doctors could show that they followed \u201cclinical guidelines\u201d for the treatment of medical conditions.\n\nAnd under the legislation, it would be easier for doctors to enter into private contracts with Medicare beneficiaries. Such contracts allow physicians to opt out of Medicare\u2019s strictures and charge more than the amounts normally allowed.\n\nThe bill would eliminate the federal health insurance exchange, through which millions of people have obtained subsidies for the purchase of insurance coverage. States could contract with private entities to set up websites that resemble HealthCare.gov, but only to provide information on insurance plans\u2019 prices and benefits and their networks of doctors and hospitals. The new websites could not directly enroll people in private plans or in Medicaid.\n\nFrom his days as a Georgia state senator, Mr. Price, now 62, has been a voice for doctors, and if confirmed by the Senate, he could use his post as health secretary to protect fellow physicians in myriad ways. As health secretary, he would have real power, not only through his influence with Congress but also through the regulatory levers of the Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services, a part of the Department of Health and Human Services.\n\nHis bill says that doctors would generally be \u201cexempt from the federal antitrust laws\u201d when they engage in contract negotiations with health insurance plans over the terms of their service. In many cases over the years, the federal government has accused doctors of violating antitrust laws in their dealings with insurers.\n\nThe legislation would also offer federal grants to states for \u201chigh-risk pools\u201d to subsidize insurance for people who might otherwise have difficulty finding coverage in the open market because of \u201cthe existence or history of a medical condition.\u201d\n\nSuch pools have not worked so well in the past, and Douglas W. Elmendorf, a former director of the Congressional Budget Office who is now dean of the Kennedy School of Government at Harvard, expressed doubts about their effectiveness.\n\n\u201cHigh-risk pools sound clever,\u201d Mr. Elmendorf said Tuesday, \u201cbut why would isolating the sickest people be expected to reduce the cost of insuring them?\u201d\n\nMr. Price\u2019s bill would also allow insurers licensed in one state to sell policies to residents of other states, a change that Mr. Trump often advocated in the campaign.\n\nBeyond the possible loss of coverage gains, Mr. Price\u2019s bill would be controversial for other reasons. One particularly contentious provision would limit the amount of tax-free coverage that workers could receive from their employers. The limits would be set at $8,000 for coverage of an individual employee and $20,000 for family coverage, with adjustments for inflation in later years.\n\nDemocrats are preparing for a fight. Blame the Enlightenment. Blame Reddit. Whatever did it, nearly all of us now recognize magic as the work of expensive gadgetry, ordinary fraud and thousands of hours of solitary practice \u2014 time a magician might instead have spent bettering the world. Or at least meeting women.\n\nBesides, in this political moment, who wants to feel bewitched, bothered and bamboozled?\n\nA lot of us.\n\nLast week I saw two magic shows: Jason Bishop\u2019s \u201cStraight Up Magic\u201d at the New Victory Theater and \u201cThe Illusionists: Turn of the Century\u201d at the Palace Theater. The first was an introduction to conjuring for the elementary-school set and their credulous parents, the second a tribute to the legerdemain of times past. At each it was pleasant to murmur \u201cHow did he do that?\u201d about something as anodyne as a spectacular levitation.\n\nMr. Bishop is a New Jersey native who taught himself magic from books while he shuffled from one foster home to the next. With his motorcycle jacket and frayed jeans, he cultivates a tough-guy persona. But he\u2019s a nice kid trying to look like a mean one, his rebel-without-a-cape pose undercut by a goofy smile and some cheerful self-deprecation: \u201cI can melt right through jail bars, but I can\u2019t get my hands on \u2018Hamilton\u2019 tickets.\u201d It\u2019s also a relief to see a beautiful assistant (Kim Hess) favoring comfortable athleisure in place of spangled lingerie.\n\nIn a brief close-up routine, shared with the audience via live video, there\u2019s real elegance and even wit in the precision of his gestures, the agility of each finger. But Mr. Bishop\u2019s enthusiasm is for larger illusions, which he performs with only moderate bombast (though it wouldn\u2019t kill him to mellow out his music cues). He can teleport his assistant, he can vaporize a dog, he can walk through a wall, he can escape a locked box, he can tell some pretty funny jokes about Chris Christie and Halloween candy. He can astound, especially if you\u2019re one of the more junior audience members: \u201cThat\u2019s not real!\u201d gasped the 6-year-old pal I\u2019d brought with me.\n\nMore gasping could be heard at \u201cThe Illusionists,\u201d altogether a bigger, blingier and kitschier enterprise, but still a less grandiose occasion than the first two seasons of this ensemble evening. This time the enchanters favored the kinds of acts, and kinds of personal grooming, that were popular a century ago. This did not translate into scrupulous attention to historical accuracy \u2014 the evening begins with a videotaped sequence. It did, however, make for some nifty facial hair.\n\nAs music played, some live and some recorded, a few lavish set pieces unfurled, like a woman sawed in half (a fine trick accessorized with some graceless sex jokes) and an escape act featuring handcuffs, a burning rope and some very pointy spikes. This was performed by Jonathan Goodwin, the Daredevil, who later returned for a swell and far simpler bit with a bullwhip and a flower that apparently had it coming.\n\nMost of the evening\u2019s bright spots were quieter, less bedazzled affairs, like a marionette act from Justo Thaus, the Grand Carlini, or a merry linked rings routine from Charlie Frye, the Eccentric, or some dumb jokes from Dana Daniels, the Charlatan. \u201cYou\u2019re a magician\u2019s dream, kid,\u201d he said, when a youthful volunteer replaced his chosen card right on top of the deck. Then she had to repeat it. Their mind-reading act was top-notch, their patter a notch lower.\n\nBut the patter doesn\u2019t really matter. That\u2019s the thing about magic. Even when it\u2019s performed with something less than perfect flair or fluency, even when we know it to be entirely fake, it accesses fantasies we all have \u2014 to flee, to fly, to see, to survive what seems impossible. They won in 2011 and lost in 2013. Holliday also helped the Colorado Rockies to the World Series in 2007, when the Boston Red Sox won in a sweep.\n\nThe agreement with Holliday came just as baseball\u2019s winter meetings were about to kick off on Monday outside Washington, and it allowed the Yankees to shift their focus toward pitching. They are expected to pay special attention to the bullpen; three closers with strong track records are on the free-agent market: Aroldis Chapman, Kenley Jansen and Mark Melancon.\n\nLast winter, the Yankees were conspicuously the only team in baseball not to sign a free agent, though they did add Chapman and Starlin Castro through trades.\n\nYankees General Manager Brian Cashman said last month at the general managers\u2019 meetings that he expected to make few additions to the lineup, even though the Yankees\u2019 offense went through a severe decline last season. Megan Urry is a former president of the American Astronomical Society and the first woman to head the physics department at Yale University. Recently inducted to the National Academy of Sciences, she has published pioneering studies on the role of black holes in the formation of galaxies.\n\nMore than 20 years ago, Dr. Urry helped organize 200 scientists who issued the Baltimore Charter for Women in Astronomy, which declared that \u201cwomen should not have to be clones of male astronomers in order to participate in mainstream astronomical research.\u201d\n\nMore recently, she announced that scientists with a record of sexual harassment would no longer be welcome at the astronomical society\u2019s meetings. We spoke at her New Haven, Conn., office. A condensed and edited version of the conversation follows.\n\nQ. Why was the meeting ban necessary?\n\nA. We had a situation where we had young female astronomers reporting conference experiences that were very different from those of their male counterparts. The men came and used the event to network. The women came and sometimes found themselves worried about people propositioning them.\n\nI wanted to make clear what our values are. I said straight out, \u201cLook, our meetings are not \u2018The Dating Game.\u2019 This is a professional environment. Act professionally.\u201d\n\nWas there much sexual harassment at astronomy conferences?\n\nProbably no more than at other corners of academe. It\u2019s a problem all over. I suspect we\u2019re hearing more about astronomy because our young people are complaining more.\n\nListen, two years ago, there was a survey published about scientific disciplines where people do field work. A high level of harassment was reported. For the men, the approaches came mainly from peers. The women reported being hit on by their superiors.\n\nThat\u2019s extremely troubling because in academia your career is in the hands of your adviser. If he\u2019s approaching you for sex, you\u2019re in trouble. There\u2019s generally no path forward in academe for the woman who has a powerful antagonist angry at her.\n\nWhat\u2019s your own story? Did you always want to be an astronomer?\n\nNo. In high school, I was the girl who was good at everything \u2014 English, history, math, science. For a while, I thought I might like to become the first woman president. A female chemistry teacher counseled me to think about a scientific career. She said I thought like a scientist because I was always comparing things to one another. I think it was my father, a science professor at Tufts, who suggested astrophysics.\n\nIn those days, the 1970s, I thought I\u2019d be able to do anything with my life. I chose physics because it was clean and elegant. I didn\u2019t go through that thing that often happens to teenage girls where they fall off of the cliff of self-confidence and get discouraged about science. The discouraging things happened later on.\n\nWhen? \n\nIn graduate school and later, when I was a postdoc at M.I.T. Before then, there was a lot of discrimination around me, but I had rationalizations. If I didn\u2019t see other females in advanced classes, I thought, \u201cMaybe no other woman wants to do this.\u201d I now think my attitude was laughable.\n\nWhen I got to M.I.T., in 1984, there were very few women full professors, maybe two. I was the only female postdoc at the Center for Space Research. It was there that I first started examining the environment I had chosen. I could see that there were plenty of young women starting out in STEM [science, technology, engineering and mathematics] fields, though the higher one went, the fewer the women.\n\nWas the atmosphere at M.I.T. discouraging to your own ambitions? \n\nThe guy who hired me was wonderful, but there were very few women. I remember a male assistant professor once telling me I\u2019d have an easy time obtaining employment once I finished, because affirmative action \u201cgave women a big advantage over more qualified men.\u201d\n\nIn 1990, when I got my first faculty position, I saw more sexism. This was at the Space Telescope Science Institute, on the Johns Hopkins campus. At the time, around 15 percent of the Ph.D.s in astronomy went to women. Yet, there were only three other woman among 52 faculty members.\n\nWhen women applied for jobs, the guys would come hear their talks. Then, they\u2019d go, \u201cWell, I don\u2019t know how much of that was hers.\u201d And then some guy gave a talk that was vague and undocumented, and they\u2019d go, \u201cLet\u2019s try to hire him.\u201d\n\nWhy remain at such a workplace? \n\nThe two-body problem. My husband worked near Baltimore, and I wanted to be where he was. And the work itself was exciting. We did the science for the Hubble Space Telescope.\n\nI wasn\u2019t paid equally. How do I know? At a certain point, I received something called an \u201cequity raise,\u201d which signaled my pay was at the bottom of my cohort. Just about all the other women got similar equity raises. We were, as a group, at the bottom of the heap.\n\nI struggled between intellectual self-confidence and constant put-downs. Once, when I requested a raise because I\u2019d been offered tenure and a 30 percent increase at another institution, I was turned down with \u201cmaybe you\u2019re not as smart as you think you are.\u201d I cried for hours.\n\nAnother time, I applied for a job I was qualified for. A male administrator sneered, \u201cYou know what makes a leader? Someone others will follow \u2014 you\u2019re not that.\u201d\n\nI was so beaten down I didn\u2019t even argue.\n\nPeople say this is the best time in history to be doing astronomy. Is it?\n\nAbsolutely. The big news is in my own area \u2014 black holes, the center of galaxies. Last year, LIGO  [Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory] detected gravitational waves from a pair of merging black holes in a galaxy probably a billion light-years away.\n\nThe waves were, of course, interesting by themselves. What fascinated me was that the merged black holes were roughly 30 times the mass of our sun. They were, at once, too small and too big for a black hole.\n\nIt\u2019s much smaller than the supermassive black holes we\u2019ve seen in the centers of galaxies, and much bigger than the black holes that come from exploding stars. It could be something entirely new.\n\nThanks to LIGO, we\u2019ve learned that the masses of the two black holes appear to have merged. The two are measured at about 30 solar masses, which is different, smaller, from what we usually see. There\u2019s some thought that they may be remnants from the first generation of stars.\n\nFor the past 15 years, I\u2019ve been discovering where and when black holes grew. My tools have been ground and space telescopes such as Keck and Hubble. We\u2019ve learned a lot from them. The LIGO discovery suggests a new way to study the universe.\n\n Will we not need those space-based satellites anymore? \n\nOn the contrary, the Chandra X-ray telescope and the XMM-Newton X-ray telescope have taught us the most about black holes. It appears that every black hole emits X-rays. The newest space telescopes spot them instantly.\n\nThe James Webb Space Telescope, which is slated to be launched in 2018, is utterly amazing. It\u2019s much bigger than Hubble, and it will enable us to see some of the first galaxies ever.\n\n \n", "east indianapolis contention separate wins nfl teams remain mondays afc jets sub500 matchup colts south buried": "\n8:30 p.m. Eastern, ESPN  Line: Colts by 1 \u00bd\n\nOnly two wins separate these sub-.500 teams, but what a difference division assignment makes.\n\nThe Colts have as good a chance as anyone to win the A.F.C. In the best of circumstances, they believe that their donations will help a cause. Other times, they are just looking for an organization to take junk off their hands or validate their taste.\n\nThen there is the case of Jason Wolff. At the end of 2007, Mr. Wolff, who considers himself a value investor, bought 16 radio stations in the greater Los Angeles area. He did very well with them, and this year he got an offer to sell them. But after the transaction was completed, he still had one left.\n\n\u201cMy wife said, \u2018Why don\u2019t we just give it to NPR?\u2019\u201d Mr. Wolff said. \u201cMy first response was, \u2018Because we can make a lot of money selling it.\u2019 But then I thought about it. Intellectually, this felt good. This felt like a value donation \u2014 if there is such a thing.\u201d\n\nSo he called the president of KCRW, the National Public Radio affiliate in Los Angeles, and asked if she wanted his radio station in San Luis Obispo, Calif.\n\n\u201cIt was so weird,\u201d said Jennifer Ferro, president of KCRW. \u201cI didn\u2019t understand what he meant.\u201d\n\nRadio stations are generally owned by conglomerates that are not in the business of giving them to public radio stations. But in this particular case, the offer seemed stranger still: Weeks before Mr. Wolff\u2019s offer, KCRW tried to buy a station in the same area but was outbid.\n\nMs. Ferro called her board and explained what was happening. \u201cThey said, \u2018Wait, you said we weren\u2019t going to be able to buy this station.\u2019\u201d\n\nThe time between Thanksgiving and New Year\u2019s Eve is the giving season, when Americans make the bulk of their charitable donations.\n\nMost of those gifts are checks written to favorite causes. But some come in the form of orphan radio stations, junked cars, unwanted boats or collections of baseball cards.\n\nAnd many charities are more than happy to accept gently used or even broken-down goods that can be sold, like old cars. The proceeds go toward their mission.\n\n\u201cWe bring in $500,000 a year from cars,\u201d Ms. Ferro said, adding that a station in San Diego gets more than $1 million from unwanted automobiles. \u201cNormally on a pledge drive, you\u2019d get $10 a month. But that same person would say, \u2018I have this car.\u2019 Someone may only give you $400 for that car, but that\u2019s $400 to KCRW.\u201d\n\nAccording to a recent survey by U.S. Trust, a banking company that caters to the wealthy, only 10 percent of people make nonfinancial gifts \u2014 like works of art, land or collections of all sorts. More than 80 percent write checks, and half of those donations are made online.\n\nStill, more than twice as many people, the study found, give items rather than appreciated securities.\n\n\u201cI have a client who gave over a whole building, a rental apartment building, in the downturn,\u201d said Claire Costello, national practice executive for philanthropic solutions at U.S. Trust. \u201cThe charity ran the building and used the proceeds for its operations. It had nothing to do with social services.\u201d\n\nAs with any gift, advisers caution, people need to know what they want to accomplish with their donation before they make it, no matter what form it takes. And they need to understand the needs of the nonprofit.\n\n\u201cWe were involved once when someone wanted to donate a building to a small arts nonprofit,\u201d said Melissa Berman, president and chief executive of Rockefeller Philanthropy Advisors. \u201cBut the building needed so much work that it wasn\u2019t clear they could remodel a building that had so much asbestos in it. In the end, it wasn\u2019t in the nonprofit\u2019s best interest to take the building without more support than was being offered.\u201d\n\nWhen the gifts and needs match, it works well. For potential heirs, it can also ease the worry about whether to keep something or sell it \u2014 and the speculation about what mom or dad would have done.\n\nMs. Costello thought of a client who had given a sizable collection of sports memorabilia to charity, a collection that probably cost the client more than it was worth. \u201cThe charity has zero investment in it, so what do they care what it\u2019s worth?\u201d she said. \u201cThey\u2019re not looking to recoup their investment, like the collector was. It\u2019s like the junk car. Who wants the junk car? But it\u2019s valuable.\u201d\n\nMany of these donations are one-offs. You have only one radio station or so many junked cars to give.\n\nBut there are other helpful ways to give away unwanted items. Ken Shubin Stein, a doctor turned investor, started Crutches 4 Kids with his sister and brother-in-law, both orthopedic surgeons. The premise is straightforward: collect no-longer-needed crutches and send them to the developing world, where lack of mobility is a public health problem for 50 million children.\n\nMr. Shubin Stein started the charity after the 2010 earthquake in Haiti, when he saw a wave of children having legs amputated. Since then, the foundation has collected and distributed about 10,000 pairs of crutches.\n\n\u201cOne of the things about crutches is you can definitely solve the problem,\u201d he said. \u201cThey\u2019re low-cost, lightweight, and there\u2019s no lucrative black market value, so they\u2019re not likely to be stolen.\u201d There are also schools and hospitals that serve as ready-made collection points for used crutches.\n\nMr. Shubin Stein said he and his family had been paying for the organization\u2019s annual budget, which is about $100,000. They have also relied on groups like Americares and MedShare International to transport the crutches that last mile.\n\n\u201cOur landed-and-delivered cost for children\u2019s crutches is about $10,\u201d he said. \u201cIt seems like the ultimate return on investment.\u201d\n\nWhat has limited this effort, he said, is the logistics of collecting the crutches more widely and then getting them to places in need.\n\nAnd this can be a challenge for anyone or any organization that wants to give or get: It\u2019s a lot harder to get things from one place to another than it is to use a credit card to make a financial donation. This is often true in the case of a natural disaster, when people send care packages.\n\n\u201cEven if these places need things like tents or canned goods, it may often be easier for the groups that can reach the afflicted to get local merchandise than deal with customs to get goods off the plane,\u201d Ms. Berman said. \u201cYou may be creating more of a headache than it\u2019s worth when you send the canned sweet potatoes.\u201d\n\nThat is not to say that small things aren\u2019t appreciated. Ms. Berman said donating toiletries to local homeless organizations can be helpful.\n\nJames Dondero, the founder of Highland Capital Management in Dallas, came up with a solution: When his company gets expensive bottles of wine or the use of an event space as thanks for its $3 million in annual giving, it gives them to another organization in need.\n\nThe wine, for example, goes to the American Heart Association\u2019s annual wine auction. The space, in places like the Dallas Zoo or the Perot Museum of Nature and Science, goes to smaller organizations that couldn\u2019t afford to rent such space themselves.\n\n\u201cWe make large gifts, so we have access to tables, admissions and golf tournaments,\u201d said Linda Owen, director of Highland\u2019s charitable giving program. \u201cWe\u2019ve calculated that there is value in this, and we thought, \u2018How can we give nonfinancial gifts to other organizations?\u2019\u201d\n\nShe said the firm supported Education Is Freedom, which helps underserved children finish high school and get into college. It took the access it had to the Dallas Zoo and gave it to the group for its graduation ceremony, which the company also underwrote.\n\nThis matchmaking is not always easy. \u201cIt is labor-intensive,\u201d Ms. Owen said. \u201cOn one level, the easiest thing to do would be to decline these opportunities. Trump promised. But they also are likely to delay the effective date so that they have several years to phase out President Obama\u2019s signature achievement.\n\nThis emerging \u201crepeal and delay\u201d strategy, which Speaker Paul D. Ryan discussed this week with Vice President-elect Mike Pence, underscores a growing recognition that replacing the health care law will be technically complicated and could be politically explosive.\n\nSince the law was signed by Mr. Obama in March 2010, 20 million uninsured people have gained coverage, and the law has become deeply embedded in the nation\u2019s health care system, accepted with varying degrees of enthusiasm by consumers, doctors, hospitals, insurance companies and state and local governments.\n\nUnwinding it could be as difficult for Republicans as it was for Democrats to pass it in the first place and could lead Republicans into a dangerous cul-de-sac, where the existing law is in shambles but no replacement can pass the narrowly divided Senate. Democrats would face political pressure in that case as well.\n\nIt is not sheer coincidence that at least one idea envisions putting the effective date well beyond the midterm congressional elections in 2018.\n\n\u201cWe are not going to rip health care away from Americans,\u201d said Representative Kevin Brady, Republican of Texas and chairman of the Ways and Means Committee, which shares jurisdiction over health care. \u201cWe will have a transition period so Congress can develop the right policies and the American people can have time to look for better health care options.\u201d\n\nSenator Lamar Alexander, Republican of Tennessee and chairman of the Senate health committee, said: \u201cI imagine this will take several years to completely make that sort of transition \u2014 to make sure we do no harm, create a good health care system that everyone has access to, and that we repeal the parts of Obamacare that need to be repealed.\u201d\n\nBut health policy experts suggest \u201crepeal and delay\u201d would be extremely damaging to a health care system already on edge.\n\n\u201cThe idea that you can repeal the Affordable Care Act with a two- or three-year transition period and not create market chaos is a total fantasy,\u201d said Sabrina Corlette, a professor at the Health Policy Institute of Georgetown University. \u201cInsurers need to know the rules of the road in order to develop plans and set premiums.\u201d\n\nDetails of the strategy are in flux, and there are disagreements among Republicans about how to proceed. In the House, the emerging plan, tightly coordinated between Mr. Ryan and Mr. Pence, is meant to give Mr. Trump\u2019s supporters the repeal of the health law that he repeatedly promised at rallies. It would also give Republicans time to try to assure consumers and the health industry that they will not instantly upend the health insurance market, and to pressure some Democrats to support a Republican alternative.\n\n\u201cI don\u2019t think you have to wait,\u201d Representative Kevin McCarthy of California, the majority leader, told reporters this week. \u201cI would move through and repeal and then go to work on replacing. I think once it\u2019s repealed, you will have hopefully fewer people playing politics, and everybody coming to the table to find the best policy.\u201d\n\nUnder the plan discussed this week, Republicans said, repeal will be on a fast track. They hope to move forward in January or February with a budget blueprint using so-called reconciliation instructions, which would allow parts of the health care law to be dismembered with a simple majority vote, denying Senate Democrats the chance to filibuster. They would follow up with legislation similar to a bill vetoed in January, which would have repealed the tax penalties for people who go without insurance and the penalties for larger employers who fail to offer coverage.\n\nThat bill would also have eliminated federal insurance subsidies, ended federal spending for the expansion of Medicaid, and barred federal payments to Planned Parenthood clinics.\n\nBut in the Senate, Republicans would need support from some Democrats if they are to replace the Affordable Care Act.\n\nThe budget reconciliation rules that would allow Republicans to dismantle the Affordable Care Act have strict limits. The rules are primarily intended to protect legislation that affects spending or revenues. The health law includes insurance market standards and other policies that do not directly affect the budget, and Senate Republicans would, in many cases, need 60 votes to change such provisions.\n\nRepealing the funding mechanisms but leaving in place the regulations risks a meltdown of the individual insurance market. Insurers could not deny coverage, but they would not get as many healthy new customers as they were expecting. Hospitals would again face many uninsured patients in their emergency rooms, without the extra Medicaid money they have been expecting.\n\nEven a delay of two to three years could be damaging. Health policy experts said the uncertainty could destabilize markets, unnerving insurers that have already lost hundreds of millions of dollars on policies sold in insurance exchanges under the Affordable Care Act.\n\n\u201cInsurers would like clarity on the shape of the replacement plan to continue participating on exchanges if Obamacare is repealed,\u201d Ana Gupte, an analyst at Leerink Partners who follows the insurance industry, said Friday.\n\nRepublicans are hoping that Mr. Trump will be able to use his bully pulpit to lean on vulnerable Democrats up for re-election in states Mr. Trump won, such as Senators Joe Manchin III of West Virginia and Jon Tester of Montana.\n\n\u201cWhen that date came and you did nothing, if you want to play politics, I think the blame would go to work with the Trump administration on replacement legislation that would draw on comprehensive plans drafted by Mr. Ryan and Representative Tom Price, the Georgia Republican picked by Mr. Trump to be his secretary of health and human services.\n\nAny legislation is likely to include elements on which Republicans generally agree: tax credits for health insurance; new incentives for health savings The company did not specify how many programs would become available for download later, saying only that there would be \u201cmore on the way.\u201d\n\nEven in areas with strong cellular coverage, watching content offline would allow users to avoid depleting their monthly data limits, addressing a major frustration of mobile video, which eats up data ravenously.\n\nCustomers who watch only a limited amount of video before incurring overage fees with their cellular carriers are more likely to stop watching videos \u2014 an unpalatable option for media companies like Netflix that want to keep eyes glued to their products.\n\nAT&T also appears to have come to a similar conclusion. He was working on a sequel to his 2003 best seller \u201cMoneyball,\u201d which chronicled how the Oakland A\u2019s general manager Billy Beane revolutionized the sport by using statistics to evaluate players.\n\n\u201cI thought I was going to show where the revolution went,\u201d he said.\n\nMr. Lewis tracked the players for two years, but the sequel never went anywhere. Their peculiar area of research \u2014 how humans make decisions, often irrationally \u2014 has had profound implications for an array of fields, like professional sports, the military, medicine, politics, finance and public health.\n\nIt helped explain why a simple algorithm is often better than the most experienced doctors at diagnosing stomach cancer, why so many financial experts failed to foresee the implosion of the housing market, and why professional basketball teams make costly errors when picking players \u2014 in short, why people\u2019s instincts are often so wildly wrong.\n\nIn 2002, Mr. Kahneman won the Nobel in economic science, a field he had no formal training in, for demonstrating how people make decisions when faced with risks and uncertainty. (When asked if their work had any application to artificial intelligence, Mr. Tversky, who died in 1996, countered that they were more interested in exploring \u201cnatural stupidity.\u201d)\n\nWhen he began digging into their research, Mr. Lewis found an even more compelling story about a fertile intellectual partnership that ended too soon, when Mr. Kahneman and Mr. Tversky had a falling-out over who was receiving more credit for their discoveries.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s a love story,\u201d Mr. Lewis said during an interview in Manhattan this fall. \u201cIt\u2019s this bromance of such great intensity, with such fertility, and the children are ideas, the children live on.\u201d\n\n\u201cThe Undoing Project,\u201d which will be released on Tuesday, seems like a departure for Mr. Lewis, whose best-known works have largely been character-driven narratives that reveal something unexpected about the way markets work. He became fascinated with their research and their unique partnership. Four years later, he finally worked up the nerve to call Mr. Kahneman and request a meeting.\n\nIt is hard to imagine Mr. Lewis, 56, feeling skittish around anyone. A New Orleans native, he comes across as someone who is at ease in any situation, whether he is shadowing professional athletes and coaches, foul-mouthed derivatives traders or bigwigs at the International Monetary Fund. After studying art history at Princeton and getting a master\u2019s degree from the London School of Economics, he worked at Salomon Brothers selling bonds. They have worked together ever since, on more than a dozen books.\n\nUnlike many nonfiction writers, Mr. Lewis declines to take advances, which he calls \u201ccorrupting,\u201d even though he could easily earn seven figures. The setup spurs him to work harder and to make more money if the books are successful, he says.\n\n\u201cYou should have the risk and you should enjoy the reward,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s not healthy for an author not to have the risk.\u201d\n\nMr. Lewis\u2019s books have sold more than nine million copies, and three have been adapted into successful feature films. The absence of Miller, who died at 95 in 2012, is one of the great scandals of the Hall.\n\nSo the classic insider made it, the classic outsider was shunned again, and The Boss remains in his personal limbo.\n\nSelig, retired and vibrant at 82, deserves the Hall, if only because he actually does know and love the game, and has from childhood, when his mother took him from Milwaukee to New York to watch Joe DiMaggio and Jackie Robinson. His Brewers won a pennant in 1982 and later he became commissioner, having one World Series canceled when he locked up with the obdurate Donald Fehr, Miller\u2019s successor at the players\u2019 association. Later Selig did a long, slow waltz with Fehr while a generation bulked up on performance-enhancing drugs. Nobody\u2019s perfect. Still, Selig deserves to be in the Hall for bringing labor peace and prosperity, as does Miller.\n\nFor his own reasons, so does Steinbrenner, who in this bombastic age is looking more layered and more cultured all the time. Well, sometimes.\n\nIn November 1974, Commissioner Bowie Kuhn suspended Steinbrenner for two years \u2014 later reduced to 15 months \u2014 after he pleaded guilty to conspiring to make illegal political contributions and also trying to force employees of his shipbuilding company to lie to a grand jury. I\u2019ve often wondered what became of that real estate guy.\n\nIn July 1990, Commissioner Fay Vincent suspended Steinbrenner for making a $40,000 payment to a confessed gambler, Howard Spira, for damaging information about Winfield, the expensive star who had disappointed Steinbrenner.\n\nBut should these acts by Steinbrenner keep him from the Hall?
